LITERARY NOTES.
Current English Literature.
Our literature of to-day has many charac-
teristics ; but its central note is the dominant
influence of Sociology—enthusiasm for social
truths as an instrument of social reform. It
is scientific, subjective, introspective, his-
jprical, archaeological — full of vitality,
versatility and diligence ; intensely personal,
defiant of all law, of standards, of conven-
tion ; laborious, exact, but often indifferent
to grace, symmetry or color; it is learned,
critical, cultured ; with all its ambition and
its fine feeling, it is unsympathetic to the
highest forms of the imagination, and quite
alien to the drama of action. . . .
• • • •
This predominance of Sociology, the rest-
less rapidity of modem life, the omnipre-
sence of material activity, fully account for
the special character of modern literature.
Literature is no longer “bookish” —but
practical, social, propagandist. It is full of
fife—but it is a dispersive, analytic, erratic
form of vitality. It has a most fastidious
taste in form—but it often flings the critical
spirit aside in its passion for doing, in its
ardour to convince and to inspire. It is in-
dustrious, full of learning and research—but
it regards its learning as an instrument of in-
fluence, not as an end of thought. It can
work up a poem or an essay as carefully as
Mieris or Breughel polished a cabinet pic-
ture —and it can “ tear a passion to tatters,”
or tumble its note-books into a volume all in
a heap. It has no “standard,” no “model,”
no “ best writer”—and yet it has a curious
faculty for reviving every known form, and
imitating any style. It is intensely histori-
cal, but so accurately historical that it is
afraid to throw the least color of imagination
around its history. Ifrhas consummate poetic
feeling, and copious poetic gifts —but it has
now no single poet of the first rank. It has
infinite romantic resources, and an army of
skilful novelists—and yet it has no single
living writer worthy to be named beside
she great romancers of the nineteenth cen-
tury.
* * * *
What is the cause % I do not hesitate to
say it is that we have over-trained our taste,
We are overdone with criticism, we are too
systematically drilled, there is far too much
moderate literature, and far too fastidious a
standard in literature. Every one is afraid
to let himself go, to offend the conventions,
dr to raise a sneer. It is the inevitable re-
sult of uniformity in education and discipline
in mental training. Millions can write good
grammar, easy and accurate sentences, and
imitate the best examples of the age. Educa-
tion has been driven at high pressure into
literary lines, and a monotonous correctness
in literary taste has been erected into a
moral code. Tens of thousands of us can
put the finger on a bit of exaggeration, or a
false light in the local colour, or a slip in
perfect realism. The result is a photographic
accuracy of detail, a bai’ren monotony of
commonplace, and the cramping of real in-
ventive genius. It is the penalty of giving
ourselves up to mechanical culture. . . .
* * * *
In all the ages of great productive work
there were intense individuality, great free-
dom, and plenty of failures. Tom Jones
delighted the town which was satiated with
gross absurdities, some of them, alas ! from
the pen of Fielding himself. Shakespeare
wrote happily before criticism had invented
the canons of the drama, and Sir Walter’s
stories had no reviews to expose his historical
blunders. In the great romance age which
began to decline some forty years ago, there
was not a tithe of such good average work as
we get now ; criticism had not become a fine
art; every one was free to like what he
pleased, and preposterous stuff was written
and enjoyed. Ox course it cannot be good
to like preposterous stuff, and an educated
taste ought to improve literature. But it is
almost a worse thing when general culture
produces an artificial monotony, when people
are taught what they ought to like, when to
violate the canons of taste is far worse than
to laugh at the Ten Commandments.
• * • *
With a very high average of fairly good
work, an immense mass of such work, and
an elaborate code of criticism, the produc-
tion of brilliant and inimitable successes
is usually arrested in every field. Having
thousands of graceful verse-writers, we have
no great poet; in a torrent of skilful fiction,
we have no great novelist; with many charm-
ing painters, who hardly seem to have a
fault, we have no great artist; with mises-en-
scene, make-up costumes, and accessories for
our plays such as the world never saw before,
we have no great actor ; and with ten thou-
sand thoughtful writers, we have not a single
genius of the first rank. Elaborate culture
easts chill looks on original ideas. Genius
itself is made to feel the crudeness and ex-
travagance of its first efforts and retires with
shame to take a lower plaee. We are all so
fastidious about form and have got such fixed
regulation views about form, we are so cor-
rect, so much like one another, such good
boys and girls, that the eccentricities and
idiosyncrasies of the inventive spirit are
taught from childhood to control themselves
and to conform to the decorum of good
society. A highly-organised code of culture
may give us good manners, but it is the
death of genius.— Frederic Harrison, in
“ Studies in Early Victorian Literature.’’
In Brief.
“ Isban Israel ” is the title of a novel by
G. H. Cossins, of S.A. Surveyor-General’s
office, to be published shortly in London by
Gay and Bird.
Victor Daley is busy revising his poems
for publication, and the book will be issued
by Angus and Robertson shortly. A per-
manent place in Australasian literature
awaits it. It was Daley who caught the lyre
that fell from Kendall’s relaxing fingers,
and he has sung for us in a manner worthy
of his master. Since Brunton Stephens
ceased to write, there has been no Austra-
lian to match the color, and brilliance, and
tender sentiment, and delicate imagery of
Daley’s verse.
Frederic Harrison’s ‘ ‘ Studies in Early
Victorian Literature ” contain some of the
sanest, most judicious criticism printed for a
long time. There is a trace of the old fogy
in Mr. Harrison, but it does not hinder him
from being perhaps the very best contem-
porary guide to appreciations of English
literature. Churton Collins is more learned
and more thorough, hardly so suggestive,
doubtfully so sound ; Saintsbury, beside
Harrison, is a mere beguiling gossip.
K.S. : “Ido not see, a propos of Edward
Maitland’s ‘Life of Anna Kingsford,’ any
allusion to his stay in Australia. Yet is he
not tho man who came to N.S. Wales years
ago, formed a platonic friendship with the
late Mrs. , and was reputed to have
‘ immortalised ’ her in one of his books—was
it ‘The Pilgrim and the Shrine’ 9 I remember
a dear old Sydney lady telling me, with many
shakes of the head, that he was a dreadful
man who ‘ believed in free-love ! ’ Poor old
lady ! She is at rest, and so spared the
modern woman and Hill-top novel.”
The death of Lady Wilde (“ Speranza,” of
The Nation) suggests mournful thoughts of
all the other impassioned bards who wrote
their hearts into the Young Ireland uprising
—wild hearts, scattered into dust of many
lands. Old Dr. O’Doherty, in Brisbane (he
married “Eva,” another well-known writer
for The Nation), and C. G. Duffy, in Nice,
are the only remnants of “the men of ’48.”
George Robertson and Co. issue a booklet
of illustrated • examples of English vowel
sounds, by Clarence Woodhouse, which may
fill an educational niche. The compiler
slips occasionally—-as in his identification of
the sound of u in true with the sound of u in
cube.
Leslie M. Oakes writes the first story in
Geo. Robertson’s “ Cooee ” series. (Recent
Australian “series,” by the way, seem to
consist of one volume only.) ‘ ‘ Ben Tillett’s
Fortune ” is a short tale of local mining life
which exhibits a good deal of crude vigor
and some inventive capacity.
“In modern literary language, man scarcely
exists save in his extremities. For wc take
the pubes as a centre, and we thence describe
a circle with a radius of some 18 inches—in
America the radius is rather longer—and we
forbid any reference to any organ within
that circle, save that maid-of-all-work the
stomach ; in other words, we make it im-
possible to say anything to the point con-
cerning the central functions of life.”—
Havelock Ellis.
“ Women, Love, and Life,” by William
Platt (Lond : Chas. Hirsch), is an unusual
book. One opinion of it is that it is “a rich
book—verse with the spring of the lion,
prose with sinews of steel.” That is the
author’s own opinion, printed at the end of
his essay, and so giving an excellent a priori
reason for disagreement. Numerous reasons
come a posteriori. William Platt seems to
have a soft spot in his cerebellum, and his
performance—a collection of thoughts on
sex-relationships—is mainly a tissue of erotic
incoherencies considerably more hysterical
than Whitman’s “ Children of Adam.” The
author’s intentions are apparently good ; his
inventions have occasional suggestive value ;
and his conventions —well, he hasn’t any.
And the gist of him is nothing like so new
or so true as he seems to think.
The New Review, distancing competitors,
prints a short story by Stephen Crane, whose
“Red Badge of Courage ” has won him a
rare literary vogue. It is a psychological
study of the effects of fear, with the re-
semblance of style and matter to Ambrose
Bierce’s more marked than ever. But
Bierce’s work—in “The Man and the Snake,”
for example—is more impressive, more con-
vincing. Again it is to be complained that
Crane’s brilliant epithets and similes do not
really give a vivid mental picture. They
seem always detached from the subject,
whose outlines are obscured in the dazzle,
and shimmer, and glare. You can’t see the
moon for the halo..
“The Red Badge,” by the way, isn’t
Crane’s first book, though it is the first to
make him known. He has published a col-
lection of verse, “The Black Riders,” and
The Bookman says he has a warm admirer
in Kipling.
According to Robert Sherard, the French
are as proud of Paul Verlaine as the English
of Tennyson, yet the most he ever received
in France for a poem was five francs.
Zola’s “ Rome ” will reach Austialia about
the beginning of July.
H.C.B. : Thanks ; but Heine (and especi-
ally “Es war ein alter Konig”) has been
done so often that you couldn’t detect spuri-
ous translation-coin. Can you lay hands on
a good, short, recent piece suitable ?
Frederic Harrison : “ The world is grow-
ing less interesting, less mysterious, less mani-
fold, at any rateto the outer eye. . . . Magnifi-
cence, squalor, oddity, historic and
picturesque personalities grow rarer year by
year. Everybody writes a grammatical letter
in conventional style, wears the clothes in
fashion, and conforms to the courtesies of
life. It is right, good, and wise : but a
little dull. . . . We live longer, but
in quiet, comfortable, oru. r'v ways. This
is not at all injurious to morality, politics,
industry, science, philosophy, or religion. It
is not necessarily injurious to poetry, at least
of the lower flight. But it is adverse to high
art. And it is asphyxiating to romance.’
Reprints.-Old and New,
Paul Kruger.
Now Christ thee save, Paul Kruger 1
Now Christ thee save from harm ;
And may the God of Joshua
Bear up thy strong right arm.
May He who fought the battles
Of all thv hero sires,
When Orange William kindled
The blaze of Freedom’s fires ;
May He whose stern war-angel
The English pirate smote,
When Cromwell’s bloody talons
Were crooked at Holland’s throat;
May He who cast his aegis
O’er flooded dyke and dam,
When down ’mid roaring billows,
Went France’s oriflamme;
May He defend the children
Who kept the fathers’ cause,
Who raised the ocean ramparts,
And bade the spoiler pause.
No guardian surges round them
Laugh now the foe to scorn,
But swelling thews and sinews
Of men to freedom sworn ;
And breasts with Right thrice-plated
And hands that love the sword,
And hearts that count most holy
The drops for freedom poured ;
And brains that guide in wisdom
The arms that strain to smite;
And Mind, o’er all victorious,
That leads the van in fight;
And o’er them, and about them,
The rapture of a world
That hails the snapping fetters,
The tyrant downward hurled ;
Yea, o’er them, and about them,
The prayer of every slave.
The hopes of every champion, **
The blessings of the brave.
Lo ! these shall be the bu’warks
No redcoat robber scales,
Till once again his banner
Before the Dutchman’s trails !
Then bring the new-born infant,
The balie iu glory sent,
From War’s red font baptismal
His shining brow besprent;
And hold him well, Paul Kruger 1
And shield him with thy life,
And for his sake he hallowed
That bloody sweat and strife 1
And joy shall lash the pulses
O freeman and of slave,
To see the Young R public
Throned on a despot’s grave.
Then Christ thee keep, Paul Kruger 1
Then Christ thee keep from harm;
And may thy deathless story
Nerve many a patriot’s arm.
Ah ! Christ thee keep, Paul Kruger!
And shrine thee in the skies ;
No fairer spirit walketh (
The fields of Paradise;
No brighter Star hath heaven
’Mid all the flaming band,
Than he who lives and labors
And dies—for Fatherland.
—Fanny Paknkll, in the Boston Pilo», Jtm. S,
1881.
A Mamory.
Oh, the sweet dried lavender !
Oh, the more than scent in it 1
The butterflies and bees astir,
The pipe of linnets pent in it 1
Brick and smoke and mire have fled—
Time and space between drop dead—
Oh, the sweet dried lavender !
I can hear the pigeons whirr,
I can count the quarters chiming,
I can watch the ivy climbing
Clinging close from eave to basement,
Clasping, shadowing all the casement.
Within, against the raftered wall,
The oaken press stands black and tall,
I see its folded linen store
Gleam athwart its open door—
I smell the lavender, fresh-dried,
Strewing all the shelves inside.
Unmade is yet your shroud, mother—
Not yet you are in heaven —
You count the sheets aloud, mother,
And smooth and lay them even.
Your jingling keys, with music low,
Measure your steppings to and fro ;
And sorting, piling, still you croon
Some soft, half-uttered cradle tune.
Oh, the sweet dried lavender !
1 hear the wise old tabby purr,
Curled on the window-sill asleep,
When winter sunlights slant and creep.
I hear, without, familiar babel
Of turkeys at the barn-door,
I, perched upon the kitchen table
In socks and pinafore.
My head is all a golden mop;
Upon my cheek the round tears drop;
The frosty morning weather nips
My nose and toes and finger tips.
Mother, so quick you leave your sheets !
The shelf of sugars and of sweets
So well you rifle for my 'meal,
Almond and fig and candied peel!
You clap my little palms, mother —
You kiss away their cold—
You take me in your arms, mother—
And I am five years old.
MAY PROUIN.
A Contributors’ Commentary.
Maeterlinck and Rossetti.
Writes M.L.M. : I think that Maeterlinck, the
once-vaunted “ Dutch Shakespeare,’’ who is little
or nothing if not imitative, has filched the germ-
inspiration of his “ Et s’tl revenait” from Ros-
setti, and indirectly therefore from Hamlet. (In
“Princess Maleine” he shows further acquaint-
ance with Hamlet.) Ophelia’s snatch of ballad
song, “How shall I my true love know?” was
developed by the poet-painter in the following
verses, under the title
An Old Song Endf,d.
" How shall l your true love know
t rorn another one?”
" By his cockle-hat and staff,
And his sandal shoon.”
•• And what signs have told you DOW
That he hastens home?”
•* Lo ! the spring is nearly gone.
He is nearly come.”
“ For a token is there nought
Say, that he should bring ? ”
“ He will bear a ring I gave,
And another ring.”
“ How may I, when he shill ask,
Tell him who lies there?”
" Nay, but leave m,v face unveiled,
And unbound my hair.”
Uan you say to me some word
1 shall say to him ?
Say I'm looking in his eyes,
Though my eyes are dim.
* * * •
The key-note of the ballad as revived bv Ros-
setti is an intense, almost bold, simplicity of
rhythm, rhyme, and expression. This simplicity,
the outcome of labor, knowledge, consummate art,
deceives t..e ordinary poetry-reader into the belief
that he himself might have written so. II would
yield reverent admiration for the workman-hip of
Lancelot and Elaine, and smile toleratingly at
such a poem as Rossetti’s marvellously dramatic
ballad, “Sister Helen,” seeing nothing but child’s
play in
“ Why did you meet yon waxen man,
Sister Helen?
To-day is the third t-ince you hegan.”
“ The time was short, yet the time ran,
Little Brother.”
“But if you have done your work aright,
Sister Helen,
You will let me play, for you said I might.”
“ Be very stili in your play to-night,
Little Brother.”
• * * •
Another word about translation. A ballad is
perhaps the most difficult form of poetry to trans-
late into verse. The difficulty lies in the absence
of epithet, in the bare, unavoidable substantives
that have often no synonyms, yet refuse to accom-
modate their syllables to the metre chosen. In
“Et s’il revenait” there is no adjective until the
fourth verse. In the adjective lies the transla-
tor’s sal vation. He can always find synonyms of
different syllables, and adjust his metre thereby.
The Bush Story.
Writes R. A.F. : Re J.H.G. and the bush story.
Do not absence of acoessory scem-ry and frill
make this more true and more dramatic? The
bushman does not travel in tourist’s knickors
looking for the picturesque. When making his
way up a coastal gully he sniffs the scented gum,
and sees on the ridges the white shaft tower high
above its black neighbor, the ironbark, and under
both the grass-tree’s pendent green blades. The
beauty of the fern, shaded by granite boulders
over which threads of water shoot, appeals to him
as to others ; but it is not his reason for being
there. Tropical rams have left water for prospect-
ing ; and, naturally enough, he talks and writes
only of the tunnel and shaft. The Western man
watches thecluuds fly over siiver-gray downs driven
by ever restless winds, and miles away sees the
line of timber that marks a creek ; but he is on a
muster, and incidents on the clay-pan where
cutting-out is going on are the things that engross
him. The bushman is in direct and naked con-
flict with nature, and the revenges she takes upon
him by flood or drought, caving drive or falling
tree, are his subject. Art or no art, here is the
dominating point of view for him. W~hen the
bushman beoomes “introspective,” he lands in
the lunatic asylum of this particular province
(Queensland). He is no nearer a problem-play or
■ex-novel than were the early Welsh bards. His
compass is short, but if he reaches beyond it he
strikes a false note. Still, perhaps, a savage in
literature, would he look any better in a trade
shirt ?

When you go out shopping, consider, think
ot the money in your pocket, and take account
ot the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation m the form of a pretty show ; there is
-ts, whose wiles are innumerable ; and 0,
who says that he is the man for your money •
and so on, throughout a list as long as
(xeorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

A Maoriland steer, 18 months old, died,
and at the post-mortem, were found five balls sin.
in diameter, and very like emu eggs in appear-
ance. Examination showed the balls were of
human hair, which the beast had swallowed while
grazing in a paddock at the back of a barber’s
shop.

The Wheels of the System.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Where is God, whilst all around us sounds the
jarring of the wheels,
W hen the cry of human anguish starwards thro’
his glory steals ?
there is neither hope nor pity underneath the
moving wheels.
Woe to him who slips and falters whilst the wheels
are moving on !
Woe to him who stays to breathe him when the
goal is nearly won !
There they lie—and lie for ever—over whom the
wheels have gone 1
O, my brothers, draw we nearer to the dream the
poet sings ?
War, red war, and rapine ruletli underneath the
shows of things.
Underneath the Masque of Mercy there are whips
of many stings.
Here in silence, reft of slumber, with sad heart I
dream and doubt,
Star by star the night is waning, star by star the
night goes out;
But the endless strife of all things ceases not within,
without.
Beat by beat the cold light groweth, beat by beat
the morn comes in
With his crimson robes about him like a royal
Paladin ;
But the endless strife of all things ceases not with-
out, within.
O er the peaceful face of Nature smiles serene the
gracious sun,
And men smile and hide their tactics when the
battle has begun.
dear the clumsy masks asunder and behold what
things are done !
For the wheels go on for ever, crushing thro’ the
human hives,
And the goal the victor reaches rests upon a million
lives,
And the motive shall not profit: it is only Power
survives !
Where weak women starve and sicken, dying in
the paths they trod,
Where strong men are bent and broken underneath
the System’s rod,
Will you smile and prate and tell me, “ This is
still the will of God ” ?
But I hear like distant thunder welling deep from
out the sky,
Tortured with the grief of ages, an exceeding
bitter cry:
‘ ‘ There is none can stay them ever, were he
mightier than I.”
“ Deeper laws than Love are hidden in the power
that runs through this.
All the fiery wheels of Heaven through the seas of
ether hiss,
Star and sun and planet rolling onward through
the black abyss.
“Wail no more, O fellow workers, for the aid He
fails to lend.”
Stricken with a deathless sorrow for the ills He
cannot mend,
God, the Worker, fights in silence for the good He
cannot send.
“Not the Lord of Love, creator of all grief, and
pain, and crime,
But a god-like soul ennobled, battling for a goal
sublime,
Thro’ the bloodshed of the asons forward to the
happier time.
“ Thine the world to mould and make it free for
all from rim to rim,
Thine to fight and toil and triumph over every
problem grim,
To create and cure and conquer, working onwards,
on with Him.
“ Face to face with iron systems, face to face with
endless odds,
Where the wheels of Heaven forever race beneath
His chariot-rods,
Soul of Man, whate'er thy sorrow, is thy burden
more than God’s ? ”
Queensland.
GEO ESSEX EVANS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 4,189 G.
Chamberlain on Federation.
Mr. Chamberlain said that it was essential that they
should seek for Imperial Federation on the line offerin'*-
the least resistance, and which would, at the same time”
establish common interests and obligations in the direc-
tion of defence and trade. This having- been accom-
plished, they could then create a representative authority.
It was imperative, however, that they should approach
the commercial side of the question. The colonies had
taken the initiative in this particular at the Imperial Con-
ference held at Ottawa.
The systems of commercial union hitherto suggested
had, Mr. Chamberlain said, offered Great Britain inade-
quate advantages for the loss and risks involved. The
dispatch of the Marquis of Ripon, the late .Secretary of
State for the Colonies, declaring that Imperial Federation
was impossible, did not debar the favorable consideration
of the question. Mr, Chamberlain thought, however,
that a better proposal would he the establishment of a
system of Freetrade throughout the Empire with revenue
duties only, This would probably lead to a satisfactory
scheme of Federation. The advantages of Freetrade
throughout the Empire were so enormous that the
colonies would be bound to carefully weigh and consider
the whole question. If the principle were once accepted,
It would then be reasonable for the Imperial Government
to convene a Council of the Empire to formulate a
definite scheme. —Cablegram,
The above represents the idea of Imperial
Federation as it presents itself to the shop-
keeping soul of J. Bull. It is propounded
by Chamberlain, the Birmingham screw-
maker, the essentially commercial man of
the Government. The British
commercial man of to-day lives in a perpetual
search for “new markets.” His own market,
under the Foreign-trade regime, is overrun
by the manufacturers and agriculturists, of
Continental Europe and America ; and, while
German and Belgian goods are swamping his
own cities, the British maker is hunting for
customers on the upper waters of the Nile
and the Congo, and around the Nyanzas and
the Mountains of the Moon. He can’t keep
hold of the customer in the next street, but
his energy in looking for the customer in
China or Borneo is unlimited. Chamber-
lain’s idea of commercial Federation is an-
pther phase of this constant search for new
markets. For many years past the depend-
encies of the Empire have been largely in-
creasing their imports, but the increase has
been principally in French, German, Bel-
gian, American, and other products, and the
demand for British goods has been practically
stationary. Chamberlain’s remedy for this
state of things, if the cablegram expresses
his views correctly, is beautifully simple and
effective. It is practically an agreement that
the “colonies ” are to buyfrom Bull, whether
it pays them to do so or not. In all points
of difference which hinder this beautiful con-
summation, the “ colonies ” are to give way ;
they are to surrender everything, while J.
Bull surrenders nothing ; and everything is
to be fixed just as J. Bull would like it to be.
* * * *
At present Great Britain travels on Foreign-
trade, or rather revenue tariff, principles
because she believes that these principles are
best for her interests, and for no other reason.
The self-governing provinces mostly travel
on protective principles tor exactly the same
reason. Before any commercial union of the
Empire can be arrived at there must be a
common fiscal system, and Chamberlain
suggests, as a simple and handy method of
arriving at this agreement, that the
“colonies” should adopt J. Bull’s system in
its entirety. Britain built her manufactures
and her commerce by many years of Protec-
tion, but for the sake of symmetry Australia
uid Canada are not to do likewise because
Britain has herself finished doing like-
wise. Also, Britain, as a country which
has a vast income from money lent abroad-,
is very differently situated from Aus-
ralia, which is drained by paying interest
>n money borrowed abroad. Britain is
protected from the aggressively cheap -
1 ibor nations the Hindoos, Chinamen,
md Japanese—by many thousands of miles
of sea-carriage, while Australia has them
practically at her door ; but these circum-
stances are by no means taken into account.
Britain did not expose her manufacturing
industries to the competition of all the
vorld until a long period of Protection
had helped to put her far ahead of all
:ompetitors. And, further, Britain, as
he principal carrier of the world’s com-
nerce, makes a large indirect profit out
f the Foreign-trade system which partly
aakes up for- the direct loss, for almost all
he cheap labor products which are humped
rom the uttermost ends of the globe to com-
»ete with her own workers pay toll to her on
he way in the shape of sea-freights, while if
Australia adopted Britain’s fiscal policy her
inports would pay freights not to herself,
>ut to Britain. Therefore, even if Freetrade
s good for Britain, which is doubtful, it no
nore follows that it is good for Australia,
vhich has not one circumstance in common
vith the alleged mother-country, than the
act that Britain requires a big navy proves
hat Switzerland, which has no sea-coast,
vants one also.
* * ♦ *
If the brief cablegram correctly repre-
sents Chamberlain’s scheme for the com-
mercial union of the empire, that scheme
practically, as regards Australia, boils
down as follows. Australia is to open
its ports to British, German, Chinese,
oid all other manufactured products,
and thereby wipe out its infant indus-
ries, furnish J. Bull with a magnifi-
cent market, and give him also the profits to
>e derived from the work of carrying this
great influx of goods across the ocean. In
return, J. Bull will continue to take Aus-
tralian wool, wine, &c., so long as he can’t
get them cheaper elsewhere, which he must
do in any case because his cold and narrow
isle doesn’t produce these things in anything
like sufficient quantity. Australia, for the
sake of falling in line with Britain, is to be
swamped by cheap Chinese and Japanese
goods (filtered through Hong Kong and
Singapore), from which Britain is largely
protected by its immense sea-distances,
and Britain is to be paid for carrying
these goods. Britain and the protected
provinces of Australia all follow their
own interests in their fiscal policy; for the
sake of uniformity these protected provinces
are to surrender what they believe to be their
interests on Britain’s unsupported assurance
that it will be well for them to do so. The
idea of Britain reversing her policy for the
sake of uniformity on, say, Victoria’s assur-
ance that it will be well for her to do so
doesn’t even enter into the calculation. It
is Britain, not Australia, which desires the
Customs Union ; nevertheless, Australia is
to make great sacrifices to gain what it doesn’t
want, while Britain gets what it does want
gratis. Altogether, there is a whole-souled
one-sidedness about Chamberlain’s ideas of
uniformity which is eminently British, and
his idea of reciprocity seems to be that
Australia should reciprocate while Britain
doesn’t.
* * * *
There is one way, and only one, by which
an Imperial Customs Union is possible—
whether it would be probable even in this way
is another question. If Britain is prepared to
accept a scheme by which there will be Free-
trade inside the Empire and high customs-
barriers against everybody outside, then there
would at least be a semblance of decency
about her proposals. In that case the wool
and wheat and wine and dairy produce and
frozen meat of this country would have a
long start in the British markets over the
produce of Argentina, the U.S., Russia,
Denmark, and all other dangerous competi-
tors. And, in return for this, Australia
might agree to let the British manufacturer
have the free run of the Australian markets,
seeing that his admission thereto would not
also mean the admission of the Chinaman,
Japanese, German, and all the cheap-labor
classes of the world. The Englishman works,
as a rule, moderate hours for a more or less
reasonable wage, and the Australian might,
in course of time, hold his own against him •
the Chinaman, who is prepared to work with
hands and feet and teeth for sixteen hours
per diem if necessary, is a totally different
circumstance. The contract would have to
apply to goods only ; the dark-skinned
races of the Empire might, possibly, be
permitted free access for their produce
to every part of the imperial territories
if there was any sufficient inducement
offered in return, but free access tor
themselves would be no part of the bargain.
And the bargain itself must be termma lie
on due notice by either party. Ihen tie
contract, so far as concerned Britain and
Australia, would simply be that each party
had the first claim on the markets of the
other as against all competitors from out-
side the Empire, and if the results weie
satisfactory the agreement might continue
indefinitely—or it might not, just as things
eventuated.
Such an agreement would be based on
purely business lines ; there would be no
provision for any aggressive loyalty about it,
or for any grandiloquent whooping concern-
ing “theF’iag.” Australia would be inter-
ested in the defence of Britain merely because
Britain was the most reliable purchaser of her
wool and butter, and because anything which
brought great disaster to Britain might
• destroy the league and thereby deprive this
country of its preferential rights in the wool
and butter trade. If these preferential
rights had proved of such value as to be
worth defending, then Australia might take
an active interest in the defence of Britain.
Britain would have equally good reason for
taking an interest in the defence of Aus-
tralia, as a country which gave it prac-
tically the exclusive right to supply it with
such manufactured goods as it didn’t make
for itself. Britain would lose nothing by the
bargain ; its exports to most foreign coun-
tries are already blocked by the highest tariff
barriers which these countries see lit, in their
own interests, to erect, and its condition
could hardly be made any worse in this
respect; and in any case, anything it might
lose in the matter of foreign trade would
probably be made good by the extra trade
done with the over-sea provinces of the
Empire. Also it would gain the inestimable
advantage of depending for its food supplies
on its own relatives, whereas it is now
mostly dependent on more or less reliable
enemies. The provinces would probably
gain as much as they lost or a little more—
at all events the balance would be about as
even as anything in this world is likely to be.
* * * *
So far, however, there appears to be no
definite reason why Australia should want
to take any chances by fooling around with
any Imperial Customs union, however per-
fect it may seem before it is tried. But if
anything of the kind ever does eventuate it
must be something wholly different from
Chamberlain’s proposals, or those of any
other British politician up to date. If
the gains and losses can’t be evenly
balanced and any sacrifice has to be made
for the sake of union it is Britain that
must make it; it is Britain, not Aus-
tralia, that desires a Customs union, and
in trading matters it is the person who wants
a thing that has to pay for it—not the one
who doesn t want it. * There is no earthly
use in appealing to Australia to federate
commercially on suicidal terms for the glory
of the empire—it is Britain’s empire, not
ours. Also, it is no use putting anything in
the negotiation about the noble traditions of
“ the Flag ” ; Australia doesn’t own the flag
and can t be expected to take anything more
than a passing interest in it. The Chamber-
lain variety of Customs Union, in fact—
always providing that the cable-liar has
correctly represented it —is too wildly
grotesque to be seriously considered by
any sane being. It is practically that
Australia should give' up its fiscal policy, a
large part of its revenues and almost all its
manufactures—that it should throw open its
ports to all the cheap products of Asia and
Europe, for even if it shut out the goods of
Germany, China and Japan, freetrade within
the empire would mean that these goods
could come in via Britain, or Hongkong,
or Singapore and that, in return, it
should get just what it has already, and what
it would have in any case. The average
British politician has so vast an idea of the
interests of England as to be incapable of
realising that any other interests can exist
anywhere to any serious extent, and Cham-
berlajn seems to have got this disease as
bactiy as his contemporaries.

The Coffin-Ship Complication.
bv E SAVGSTFP^ n h he ? ip F ing trade > as revealed
y > n&. TERb board of enquiry—altogether
apart from the question of the seaworthiness or
otherw.se of the Wendouree or any other
vessel seems to be something like this • In-
tense competition keeps fares and freights in
sUnJanHl If™ " hat WO « ld be th^bte
standard if the proper amount was spent on
repairs and renewals. Very often the ship
owners, owing to spasmodic outbreaks of
fierce competition, can’t afford to do repairs
and to discard obsolete ships, so they run their
floating graveyards as long as they will keep on
top of the water, and cheerfuffy damn P the
crews whose lives are thereby put in jeopardy
1 16 ™* Pl ne insurance companies pracKfc
coerce their surveyors into passing every vesS
that will float, and insure it accordinSv
Almost every shipping co. seems to have one or
' or mor e coffin-vessels at the tail-end of its
bst engaged m carrying coal or other freight
and if the insurance co. doesn’t insure them it
risks losing the insurance on the rest of ttp
fleet, therefore-as another res b of
tion-the office which didn’t insure theS
miirlit oo \*r£.n *li6 cofim-
e sel might as Hell go out of the business.
The law makes the surveyors practically
the servants of the shipowners, and the
shipowners might as well coerce the sur-
veyors into passing rotten vessels as not •
even if they were absolutely honest
they would never get credit for it while
they are in their present false position. The
Marine Boards are mostly composed of ship-
owners sitting in judgment on their own inter-
ests, and they might as well strain their
authority in their own favor as not—if they
were honest they would never get credit for it
owing to the false position in which they also
are placed. If all the insurance co’s would
refuse to insure old, unseaworthy craft and
their cargoes, then no one would ship goods by
them, and they would become practically out-
lawed, and most of the companies would, in all
probability, be glad to do this ; only each one is
certain that, even if it kept faith, some “scab”
office would break the agreement at the last
moment, and insure all the coffin-ships in a
lump, and get all the good business in consider-
ation of doing so, and thereby leave its com-
petitors in the lurch. If there existed any
authority which would compel all the ship-
owners to keep their vessels up to a first-class
standard, probably even the shipowners would
not object very much when they got used to
the situation. The extra expense would fall on
them pretty equally all round, and the fares
and freights would be adjusted to cover it.
But while no such authority exists the co.
which saves money by not repairing its vessels
can thereby run a little cheaper than the one
which doesn’t save money, so wherever there is
competition there is inducement to economise
by letting ships take their chance provided the
rot and the- rust-holes and the leakages and the
general old age are not visible to passengers.
Starting with the assumption that the ship-
owner and the surveyor and the insurance co.
and the various boards don’t care anything in
particular for the lives of the sailor men, the
rest follows as the natural result of Free Com-
petition. And the results are so inextricably
mixed'up that it is practically impossible to say
where the trouble commenced. It may have
started with the frantic competition of the
marine insurance companies, whose desire to cut
each other’s throats led them to cover any kind
of vessel that would float; or with the owners
who starved the repairs account in good times
to pay bigger dividends than each other, and
did the same on a larger and larger scale in bad
times, so that they could offer lower fares and
freights than each other ; or, it may have begun
partly with the shipbuilders who cut each
other’s quotations to get business, and then
supplied vessels that were weak and jerry-
built at the outset because the prices wouldn’t
run to anything better; or more likely it was the
work of all these and other influences combined.
Anyhow, the reasons for the existence of coffin-
ships seem now to be so inextricably mixed up
that unless the State interferes by handing
over the surveying and licensing of all vessels
to some absolutely independent authority, re-
form seems very improbable. The great doc-
trine of Free Competition is like a python with
its tail in its mouth. It has no visible beginning
or end, and no one can attack one part of it
without infuriating all the rest of the animal,
and to expect Free Competition to produce any
remedy for its own ill-doings is just the same
as expecting the python to swallow itself bodily
and thus remove itself out of the way.
The Deceased Wife’s Sister.
What does Hogan, self-elected member for
Australia in the Commons, mean by his De-
ceased Wife’s Sister’s Children Legitimation
Bill? Premiers Reid and Turner have cabled
authority to support it (by the way, by what
right ?), but, for all the information they have
yet received concerning it, it may contain a
repeal of the constitution, a pension to Parkes,
or a toleration of polyandry'. We are told that
it is to legitimatise in England the offspring of
such alliances, when they are valid in the
colony to which the parties belong. But such
marriages of people domiciled in the colonies
are already valid in England, and their children
are already legitimate in England. The only
possible defect is that they may not be
able to succeed to intestate real property
in England. As to such marriages between
people not domiciled in the colonies, the
bishops of the House of Lords will never
allow it, for it would simply mean pass-
ing a Deceased Wife’s Sister’s Act with a con-
dition attached that the parties must undertake
a short sea-trip to get the ceremony performed.
Even if the Bill refers to the succession to
intestate real estate, it is a hasty and ill-con-
sidered, attempt to curry favor, or to justify
Hogan s colonial pose. For in several of the
colonies and in Scotland legitimation by subse-
quent marriage prevails, and, although the
children are legitimate in England, they are
certainly in this case prevented by an absurd
and technical rule of English law from inherit-
ing land. That rule is thus expressed : “ The
English heirship required not only that the
man should be legitimate, but, as it were
porphyro genitus, born within the narrowest
pale of English legitimacy.” If Hogan and co.
were really anxious to mend what is possibly a
wrong, and had properly studied before rushing
into a Bill, they would have framed a measure
to enable certain colonial legitimate children
to succeed to English land in intestacy.” But
if the Bill so hastily assented to by Reid and
lur,ner is really what its title expresses it to
be, it will probably cast serious doubt on the
validity of the marriages where there is none
at present, and may cause by implication
serious confusion as to the status of children
)orn before the Act comes into operation.
Again, what does Hogan mean ?
The Democratic “Argus.”
Melbourne Argus is in a pathetic quandary
over the Vic. Companies Act. It has system-
atically called for vengeance on the “crook”
ankers and company “ haves ” of Smellbourne,
mainly because it suspected the Age of trying
to smother investigation in the early stages of
ie jurst on behalf of its friend Munro and
o - iers. At the same time it urged the Vic.
Oounci l to throw out the Companies Bill (which
me at the root of the evil by making


directors personally responsible for the lying
balance-sheets and unreliable reports issued by
the concerns which they profess to manage, and
prohibited them selling their own properties to
tiieir own companies, and doing many other
shady things), mainly because it was introduced
by the Turner Government which the Age
supports. Now the City of Melbourne Bank
infamy having shown the dire necessity for the
measure which the Argus recently urged
the Council to reject, that paper again demands
the lost Bill in a truculent tone of voice, and
denounces the Turner Government for not sup-
plyinf?*L 1 he present attitude of the Argus is
peculiar. It alleges: (1) That the Companies Bill
is a much-wanted reform and should have been
passed; (2) I hat the Turner Government,
which passed this Bill through the Assembly
and did its best to get it through the Council,
is wholly wrong ; (3) That the Council, which
destroyed this valuable reform, isn’t opposed to
reform at all, and any such assertion is a base
invention by the Government ; (4) That it is
really the Council and not the Government
winch longs for the Bill ; the Council wanted
to pass the Bill (which was a right thing for it
to do), and only threw it out (which was also a
right thing to do) because it arrived at “ the
fag-end of the session ” and because the Upper
House would not be “ bluffed and bullied into
passing serious measures without due considera-
tion. Of course it is obvious that some
measure must be dealt with at the tag-end of
the session if the session is to have any end at
all ; and the Council is supposed to be as intel-
ligent at the end of the session as at any other
time ; and there was abundance of time
for consideration, seeing that the Council
knew what was in the Bill for weeks
before it formally arrived ; and though the
term “fag end” sounds very expressive, it
really means no more than the fag-beginning or
the fag-middle. Also, the Council didn’t de-
stroy the Companies Bill because it arrived at
the end of the session ; it considered it leis-
urely and at great length, and rejected all its
salient clauses because, as James Service
frankly stated, they would “ make a director
responsible for everything he put his name to,”
just like every other man, and the thirty direc-
tors in the House of Landlords flatly refused to
be responsible for the truth of their own state-
ments. The Argus, in its desire to prove that
the Bill was right, and the people who destroyed
it were right, and the people who supported it
were wrong, and that it was really the people
w'ho supported it that didn’t want it, and the
people who destroyed it that longed for it with
a great, unselfish longing, gets itself into a
lamentably mixed condition. The best plea it
can put in is that the 48 Rich Property-owners
can’t be expected to care for the interests of
the country towards the end of a session, and
they certainly don’t—so far, no one has found
them caring for any interests save their own,
either at the end of a session or at any other
time.
Melbourne University Too Much.
Dear Bulletin, —Melb. Age of 10th March
has the following par. :
A circumstantial statement was made at a meeting of
the University council, held on the 3rd ult., by the chan-
cellor, Sir A. C. Brownless, to the effect that “ the
council had refused an offer of .£30,000 from Dr. Grekves
to endow a scholarship for Oddfellows and their sons, and
that the Council had also declined an offer of £20,000 from
Mrs. Cameron to endow a scholarship for pupils from the
Western district.” An anonymous correspondent, signing
himself X.Z., now writes : “How the press came to state
these amounts I do not know, but I do know that the
press is at fault.” Our correspondent ventures to correct
the chancellor's figures, alleging that the amount offered
by Dr. Greeves was only £SOO, and by Mrs. Cameron
£2500.
In The Bulletin of March 14, “J.S.R.” ac-
cuses me of misquoting Chancellor Brownless,
just as “ X.Z.” accuses the Age. It is true
that “X.Z.” does not appear to lay serious
charges about spelling “ Graves ” “ Greeves,”
&c. , but in other respects there is a striking
similarity of tone. But it is of no importance
to the issue really involved whether the Chan-
cellor wrongly put the figures, or whether
“J.S.R.” is identical with “X.Z.” The prin-
ciple allegedly obtaining, “ that every Univer-
sity prize should be open to every student,
without, distinction,” is a mere chimera, be-
cause there are hundreds of students who
are prevented by the University time table
from taking advantage of University education.
Their poverty and the University’s will pre-
vents. As to close scholarships being neces-
sarily objectionable, it is obvious that neither
of the scholarships above-mentioned would
diminish the privileges of any person in the
province, while they would increase the privi-
leges of a few, at the same time bringing in
revenue. The “class” benefited would only
get what was paid for, and the University
would only have another customer for its com-
modity. “ J.S.R.” also says :
Were the University to grant a dozen scholarships to
State scholars only, they would certainly be class scholar-
ships, inasmuch as boys and girls educated at other
schools or at home would not be eligible to compete. No
true democrat would deliberately deprive a very large
section of the population of a share in the advantages of
a public institution half of whose income comes out of the
general revenue.
“ At the end of 1892,” says Coghlan in “ Seven
Colonies, 1894 ” (p. 118), “Victoria had 2140
State-schools, with an average attendance of
141,364 scholars out of a net enrolment of
215,020, or a gross enrolment of 249,786.” The
number of Vic. children in 1891 over five and
under 15 years of age was 244,436. If the
State scholars number 215,000 out of 245,000 it
is hard to determine where we shall find that
very large section of the population that is to be
deprived of its rights by the institution of
scholarships for State-school scholars. The
very fact that the University gets half of
its income out of the State funds, should
make it decently attentive to representations
by properly authorised officials —and Minister
»! Education Peacock may be depended on to
secure this attention. My critic gives a
grotesque distortion of my position in the
following passage :
“ F.” tries to make out that the University does all it
can to keep the working man’s boy down. Does “ F.”
know that a Melb. lady offered four “ Louise ” scholar-
ships of £4O a year each for girls wishing to enter the
medical profession, who had never attended a State school?
Does he know that the University refused them point-
blank? What a howl “ F.” and his like would have set
up had those been accepted !
My letter indicated that I saw no objection to
endowing scholarships for young ladies with
strawberry marks on their left elbows, and
much less objection to permitting special privi-
leges to be bought for young men in Vic.
estern district. In fact, I would have no
objection to a special endowment (paid for by,
say, “ X.Z.”) for the benefit of “ J.S.R.”—F.
The “Unfortunate Shareholder” Again.
Chief among the impudent fictions of James
Williamson —the City of Melbourne Bank
director who stated that “no gentlemen of stand-
ing and principle” would direct banks if they
were prohibited from deciding upon their own
claims to overdrafts—was the fiction that the
money absorbed by his colleagues belonged to the
shareholders, and not to depositors. William-
son is not the originator of this glaring untruth.
It was invented even prior to the reconstruc-
tion period, and served as the excuse for the
accursed Voluntary Liquidation Act of the
James Munro regime. The boomsters’ policy
was to deny as far as possible their responsi-
bility to the real dupes of their false balance-
sheets, and throw themselves upon the mercy
of shareholders, who consisted largely—in some
cases entirely—-of themselves, their relatives,
and friends. Matthew Davies, brother of
J. B. Davies, and pious James Balfour
wept more tears for the unfortunate share-
holders in their Freehold Investment Co.
than they shed for the depositors whose
money had furnished them (the directing
shareholders) with unearned divs. The Federal
and Mercantile Bank magnates, when called
upon to apologise for little indiscretions, mar-
shalled their troops of victims, figuratively
speaking, with the shareholders in the fore-
ground hiding the depositors from view. And
when the Commercial closed its doors and re-
construction became fashionable, the “gag”
which J. Williamson lately inflicted upon
Judge Molesworth was very popular among
financial mummers. Shareholders’ money was
said to have been unfortunately lost, whereas it
was in some cases solidly represented by bricks
and mortar. All that the shareholders did, per
medium of their directors, was to build a head
office and branches with the subscribed capital.
Directors didn’t vote bricks, stonework, and
fittings to themselves and other wholesale
borrowers. They operated upon the de-
posits, the money put into the trap which
the shareholders had constructed, and which
returned handsome dividends to the builders
for many years. The City of Melbourne Bank
shareholders, as a body, will lose by the
failure of their deplorable institution, no doubt,
but a good many of them, having drawn more
out in dividends and overdrafts than they ever
put into it, are emerging from their “ misfor-
fortune ” with a profit. Calls made upon them
now will not be met. On the other hand, all
plain, straightforward depositors will be vic-
timised. They are the people to whom defunct
manager Longmuir and his surviving co-bor-
rowers owe their bank-accounts. Director J.
Williamson had better paste this fact inside
his shiny black belltopper, and read it care-
fully every time he goes to church and mutters
a preliminary prayer into his headgear.
The latest outbreak of the illuminated-address
complaint is almost unique. Some time ago a
Sydney land-shark co. bought an “estate” on
deferred payment and cut it up and sold it,
also on deferred payment. The buyers, mostly
workmen, built houses on it with their savings,
and paid their instalments pretty regularly ;
the co. took the instalments, but it paid neither
principal nor interest to the original vendor.
Therefore, there came a time—as the co. must
have foreseen —when the original vendors started
to sell the property to recover their money,
and that sale meant the ejection of all the small
buyers, the loss of their homes and all the
money they had paid, and their utter ruin.
The situation was so utterly disgraceful that
the directors of the land-shark co. set to work
at last to repair their wicked negligence, and,
after a period of agonising suspense, the
buyers were informed that some kind of a
compromise had been effected, and they
wouldn’t be robbed after all. Then a meet-
ing was held, and gorgeous illuminated
addresses were presented to the directors
because they hadn’t ruined the people who
trusted them but had only come near doing it.
The addresses, in fact, wallowed in gratitude
to the directors because their miserable negli-
gence hadn’t been so disastrous as was expected.
Minister Gould, who presented them, took the
opportunity to make remarks to the effect that
similar accidents had happened before, and that
Parliament had been asked to pass a law to
prevent people selling to other people land they
hadn’t got, and taking the other people’s money,
and then failing to deliver the article paid for
or to return the cash ; but he explained that
Parliament had decided not to interfere with
the swindle lest it should thereby “sap the
independence and self-reliance” of the com-
munity. Then everybody slobbered over every-
body else, and there were votes of thanks and
“ cheers.”
The great difficulty under which Sangster,
M.L.A., labors in proving his Australian coffin-
ship allegations is that all his witnesses are
men who depend for their bread and meat on
the goodwill of the witnesses on the other side.
In other words, whoever gives evidence for him
is thereby giving evidence against somebody
who has power to summarily deprive him of his
means of livelihood for doing it, and it is very
hard to prove any case under such conditions.
Of course, no shipowner or other employer will
“sack ” any man for givingevidence against him,
but in such cases the witnesses for the prosecu-
tion generally “get the run” one by one, very
shortly afterwards, for incapacity, negligence,
or something else, or else they aren’t wanted
owing to scarcity of work, or something hap-
pens to them by the act of God or the Queen’s
enemies in a perfectly natural way that can’t
possibly be proved as animus on the part of the
boss. In this connection one significant inci-
dent occurred last week. A certain officer
whose business it is to see that only
qualified men are supplied as seamen, and
w'ho can consequently run out any man
w'ho, in his view, is not properly qualified,
seems to have been absent from his proper busi- -
ness for a whole week to sit persistently in the
court and watch the inquiry. He didn't inter-
fere with or threaten or intimidate anybody, but
his presence was an automatic reminder to each
nautical witness that he would know him
again, and that if he liked he might thereafter
run him persistently out of every ship in the
harbor as a man who wasn’t properly qualified.
And as there was no visible reason for his pre-
sence unless he was there to take stock of the
witnesses, they naturally, when they saw him,
showed a desire to leave the court by a side-
door and be seen in those parts no more. Under
conditions like these Sangster is engaged in an
uphill fight.
In the Tagliaferro cattle-goring case, at Melb.
Supreme Court, the Judge decided that the
barrier erected to keep the cattle within bounds
was insufficient owing to the negligence of the
captain and the chief officer of the ship, and
that through this insufficiency the plaintiff
(seaman) was injured. But, as here antici-
pated, he decided that, as the captain and chief
officer were fellow-servants of plaintiff, defend-
ants were not liable for their negligence. He
therefore found for defendants “ somewhat un-
willingly, as plaintiff had been injured without
any fault of his own.” The glaring anomaly of
such a law will appear all the more clearly
when it is remembered that if the owners
themselves had personally seen to the erection
of the barrier, they would have bqen liable.
Surely if there is a case where the doctrine of
“ alter ego ” or “ vice-principal ” can come in,
it is in the case of the captain of a ship !
Some democratic sympathy exists in Westralia
for Works-Commissioner Venn, thrown over-
board by Premier Forrest as a sacrifice to the
offended people who couldn’t get rolling-stock.
No doubt Venn’s department is shamefully
mismanaged, and John Forrest told com-
plainants in effect: “Oh, yes, it’s all that
chuckle-headed Venn —what can you expect? ”
Venn stood this for a long time, but at last
grew restive and informed the public that the
complications were wholly due to Forrest,
who cut down the railway estimates after being
informed that more trucks and engines were
absolutely necessary. Forrest was indignant
that one of his subordinate worms should turn,
and when Venn obstinately refused to resign
from the Cabinet he persuaded Gov. Smith to
throw him out. As the Venn crowd is pretty
nearly as mossy a lot of old W. A. shellbacks as
the Forrest crowd, this has led to an eminently
gratifying split among the Six Families. Now
that the Tories have fallen out there is some
chance that the public may come by its own.
Dear Bulletin, —The queries of “D.F.”anent
the uses to which hospital-patients are put “ in
the interests of science ” and the “ upper
classes ” are very pertinent. It is a damnable
thing that in the case of women, suffering from
ailments peculiar to the sex, and, although only
the wives or daughters of working men, as
keenly sensitive as the daughters of luxury,
they should, in scores of cases, be debarred the
privileges of our great institutions, because they
are unable to face the ordeal of an operation
under the eyes of half-a-dozen hard-faced medi-
cal students. Even when examinations are
being conducted, and the subject is not clothed
in chloroform, the students gather about, and
the patient enjoys the felicity of being com-
mented upon exactly as if she were a flayed dog
strapped to the operating-table. I know of
several instances of sufferers preferring to con-
tinue sufferers rather than tender their bodies
to be mauled by doctors for the edification
and enlightenment of a regiment of sucking
sawbones. The preliminary examination is
usually enough and to spare.—G.S.
The Q. elections are wriggling along, and will
continue to wriggle for another fortnight, in
accordance with the tortuous policy of Boodle-
wraith. The strength of parties is now esti-
mated at—Government, 32; Labor, 7 ; and
miscellaneous, 5. Two of these latter call
themselves Opposition members ; two claim to
be Independents ; and one alleges that he is a
Farmers’ Representative. In the Southern
agricultural constituencies half-a-dozen candi-
dates have come forward this time on the
“ farmers’ representative ” ticket, ostenta-
tiously crying “a plague on all your poli-
tical houses; ” but with little luck. In the
Canadian province of Ontario a very substantial
farmers’ party was formed at last elections; and
there is quite a good chance for a similar section
in Q. politics. This year’s candidates, how-
ever, didn’t recommend themselves, being
mostly, like miller Kates, defeated by Labor at
Warwick, shifty Ministerialists in disguise.
As a result of last week’s polling, Labor
barely holds its own. The loss of the
Gympie seat was counterbalanced by a win at
Woolloongabba, but there is a distinct loss of
prestige. It takes all the merit of North Bris-
bane to make up for Townsville and Gympie.
The general issue of the elections promises to
be a Parliament almost identical with the last
in strength of parties. The western and far
northern constituencies, in which is Labor’s
chief reliance, are still to poll—and the Go-
vernment, which put them last on the list in
order that they might have no influence on
weak-kneed brethren, is well aware of it.
The Christian Commonwealth lately com-
plained that no less than nine “ridiculous
and contemptible specimens of the clergy” were
represented on the London stage, and, inasmuch
as every parson is not “a cross between a fraud
and a fool,” the editor laid it down, as a religious
fact, that all stage characters are mischievous
and misleading caricatures. This is base in-
gratitude for past favors. Dramatic authors
have done a lot towards creating an exag-
gerated notion of parsonic manliness and virtue.
A long succession of Irish prastes in Bouci-
caultian dramas were rather mischievous and
misleading—in the opposite direction to that
mentioned by the Christian Commonwealth.
The noble hero of “ The New Magdalen ” was
a curate. Dear, delightful vicars have been
bestowing paternal blessings on the heroine as
long as the oldest playgoer can remember. In
short, prastes, curates, and vicars on the stage
have been, as a rule, sixteen times more truly
Kerristian than the average soul-saver in real
life. It was time for an epidemic of ridiculous
and contemptible specimens to be administered
as a corrective.
Trial by jury is not the perfection of human
reason, but, from trial by Judge, good Lord
deliver us ! Here is an object-lesson from ’Ome.
A certain action under the “ Employers’
Liability Act,” was brought in the County
Court. At the trial, the County Court Judge
—a Judge of fact as well as of law, a Judge-
cwm-jury—held that there was no evidence to
show that defendants were liable. On appeal,
two Justices of the Queen’s Bench upheld this
decision. On some more appeal, three Judges,
the Master of the Rolls, and two Lord Justices,
upheld it again ; one Lord Justice dissenting.
On some more appeal, this time to the House of
Lords, seven Judges—viz., the Lord Chan-
cellor, an ex-Lord Chancellor, and five Lords of
Appeal—disagreed unanimously with the three-
times-affirmed decision and reversed it. The
case sorts out thus : —On one side : A County
Court Judge (theoretically an infallible Judge
of fact), two Justices of the Q. 8., the Master of
the Rolls, two Lord Justices. On the other :
One Lord Justice, the Lord Chancellor, an ex-
Lord C., five Lords of Appeal. The Goddess
of Justice should be thankful she’s blind. She
can toss up for it.
The Lormer Board has sent in its report. It
finds J’s.P. Baxter and Rapiport guilty of
corruption, by taking bribes from litigants.
Also, it finds that old Bird, the gushful, fuzzy
gentleman who met girls at a late hour, for the
purpose of giving them good advice, “adjudi-
cated in the interests of women of bad fame and
character, with whom he had, at the time,
immoral relations.” Likewise, most of the
charges of bench-packing are found proved.
The accusations brought against Lormer are
found not proven ; but the general burden of
the report is that the unpaid J.P. system is a
blot and an infamy, and that the J.P. who
isn’t paid generally pays himself in some way
or other. The Board suggests, as a remedy,
that no J.P. should be allowed to adjudicate
in the courts of Melbourne or its suburbs,
Bendigo, Ballarat, or other large towns.
Outside these towns, he will apparently
be allowed to rage as usual, simply be-
cause the State cannot afford to appoint a
paid magistrate in every separate bush hamlet.
Therefore, the question of allowing the people
to elect their own honorary “ beak,” where the
honorary “ beak ” is inevitable, again crops up.
It other words, the question is whether the
bush J.P. should be elected for a fixed term by
the people who know him and who have to
suffer from his ignorance and incapacity if they
el ect an unfit person, or if he should be ap-
pointed by an authority in the capital, which
authority doesn’t know' him, and doesn’t suffer
by him, and has a direct interest in appointing
a bad man if he is a political supporter rather
than a good man who isn’t.

This is the orthodox person all over. He never
proves or attempts to prove that his opponent
is wrong. He has no facts and no logic. If
anybody doesn’t agree with him he simply
waves his hair in the blast and says on his own
sole authority that that person will be damned.
The Bulletin will be glad to hear any facts
which its correspondent can adduce to show
that its view of the Chinese mission subject was
incorrect, but it flatly refuses to be damned by
Peter M‘Faklane or any other unauthorised
person who says that it will be chewed by
“ Bares.”
The Man that’s Dead.
(Respectfully dedicated to the directors of a certain Bank.)
The bank was broke and they all agreed
That somehow the money had fled away—
Had fled like a spirit that death had freed
From its tabernacle of earthly clay.
So they figured the lost coin up and said
That the assets were “ shook ” by the man
that’s dead.
They all agreed the accounts were mixed,
Though no one knew how it came to be ;
And no one could tell how the books were fixed
Or who was the cause of the mystery.
For these bulky tomes bound in brown and red
Had been muddled and mixed by the man that’s
dead.
They mostly had overdi’afts large or small,
Or middle-sized, as the case might be,
And these overdrafts, taking them all in all,
Were built on most shaky security.
And the board resolved that the coin it lent
To itself, and which it had “ blewed ” and
spent,
Had quite unaccountably melted and fled
Through the shameful neglect of the man that’s
dead.
The big reserve-fund was wholly gone,
Like last year’s dew on the wattle-tree ;
The capital went like a ghost at dawn,
The bills were as bad as such things could
be ;
And naught remained but a bad, bad smell,
For the big stone bank was an empty shell ;
So a sad, wet tear the directors shed
O’er the wickedness of the man that’s dead.

There is a Westralian claim called the
United Scotchman. Tautology ! The Scotchman

Mrs. Arthur Kitson, formerly of Sydney,
has recovered <£12,000 (another account says
<£1200) damages from Dr. W. S. Playfair, of Lon-
don, who divulged secrets acquired during the
course of his professional duties which were
alleged to reflect on her character. The Bulletin
doesn’t remember any other case of the kind where
a doctor was concerned.
A N.S. Wales father who went to a dance
and left five little children alone in the house with
a lighted candle—and the usual result (all burnt
to death)— is quoted as telling a reporter that “ he
lost everything he possessed in the world. He
puts down the money value of his house and
effects at <£2oo. . . . He was fearfully cut up
at the loss not only of his property —he said he
would not have cared so much for that—but his
children. This was the heaviest blow.” Some
people—or some reporters—have a queer sense of
congruity.
The despatch of an Egyptian military force
to Dongola is about the boldest piece of British
foreign statesmanship since Disraeli’s time. It
displeases the German Government. Now is
Premier Salisbury’s time for “rubbing it into”
the Germans, who have discovered that their
chance of a military gain by a war with Great
Britain is small in comparison with the certainty
of a commercial disaster. And, though France
is also furious, the mutual antagonism of France
and Germany makes the move a pretty safe one.
Q. Ministerialists are aggrieved because
aftor Eidsvold election their opponents are alleged
to have chased and maltreated Govt, voters. The
ruling Q. party has murdered (per its Kanakas and
other colored laborers) 17 men in the last three
years: it has outraged several women and com-
mitted numberless assaults per the same medium ;
it lias infected the white population with leprosy;
it has robbed thousands of their votes; it has
discreditably manoeuvred the public funds and
demoralised political life; and now its supporters
complain because some bard words and a blow or
two pass in an election row !
The railway has reached Coolgardie at last,
and the opening was celebrated by five days of
awful official drunk and a universal hiccup that
blew sand-clouds out of the town east, west, north
and south. The train with the Government on
1 Hiard bumped in only four hours late, which was
Imld to augur well for the success of the line, and
the Ministers were met by an admiring crowd
which groaned and called rut “Rats!” A great
procession of drunks, dry-blowers, unemployed,
Afghans, camels, Chows, ladies of doubtful
character, publicans, and niggers escorted the
Governor into the town, and five leading Afghans
presented him with an address. A great political
meeting was held at which it was unanimously
agreed that the Ministry was a bloated fraud, and
the rest of the proceedings were mainly hard
drinking, profanity, dog-fights, and general uproar
through the length and breadth of the city of
bagging and galvanised iron. Also, there \yere the
usual speeches about “ the resourees of this great
country ” —a phrase which should oe put down by
act of Parliament.
One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*

Sydney, March 31, 180 G.
Mr Dear Moorabtnda—
The team of (alleged) cricketing M’sP. from the
Cabbage Garden spent a festive week in Sydney,
but the ostensible “ raison ” of their jovial raid—
the cricket-match between N.S.W. and Vic. legis-
lators—was quite the most scamped item on their
programme. “Luncheon” was a protracted in-
terval, and consequently the farce-cricket was a
sur.ces de rire, at all events. From the loosely-
filled members’ stand echoes of Lauri’s famous
laugh in “ Nitoucho ” went up continuously, and
the gathering was so infectiously happy that fat,
top-hatted legislators shook their sides with appar-
ently causeless laughter.
The inter-Parliamentary cricket-match was a
most hilarious gathering—shockingly managed, so
far as the lady-visitors went (I took the neglect
as an augury that one-petticoat-one-vote is still in
the dim distance), but evidently a veritable
triumph of masculine hospitality. Perhaps the
most significant bit of by play daring the after-
noon was when a burly local M ( > . inarched into
the field with what the initiated call a “long-
sleever ” of—well, it might have been water —and
placed it on the grass within sight of the players
much as one holds out carrots to a pet horse. That
beckoning goblet supplied the keynote of the day’s
athletics.
Anrvnos politics—a deputation of Sydney tabbies
waited on Colonial Secretary lirunker the other
day, and held forth at length on their favorite
theme, “the age of consent.” The deputation
included 28 matrons and one spinster—all, appa-
rently, versed in the unsavory subject—and they
swooped d >wn under the aagis of .Mr. M'Miilan,
who said, hesitatingly that this was a matter of
great importance, but he would prefer the ladies
should mike their own representations. Then
the She-Gimletists waded in.
The deputation confessed, as a start, that it had
read the Maiden Tribute articles “ with great in-
terest.” Of course. There’s not much “spicy”
realism a “ Y ” woman misses, so long as it’s de-
scribed in a horrified but explicit tone, with names
and dates, and a parson’s picture on the top. 'I hen
a grim lady arose and remarked that they had
Come in the interests of trie lower classes more
than those of the higher—which was a tremendous
slur on the poor but virtuous, though I am willing
to believe the Giniletists would gladly exploit the
morals of the rich folks, too, if only they weren’t
sure of having gilded portals banged on their in-
ouisitive noses. It is one of poverty’s greatest
drawbacks that you are liable to have a areadful
female rushing into your humble cot and anxiously
inuuiring if your daughter is as good as she ought
to be. However, this assurance from head-quar-
ters that the social-purists mean to maintain their
masterly inactivity with regard to the morals of
I’otts Point, may be comforting news for ttas-
siety.
This seems to be the season for reforming people
off the face of the earth. The Council of the
Churches in Sydney held a conference on gambling
the other day, when one reverend youth appeared
to me to hit the bull’s-eye in saying that it was
first of all necessary for the church to cleanse her-
self, and he hoped for a pronouncement from the
heads of the church against lotteries and raffles
being permitted at bazaars. That’s just it. While
the little tin gee-gees are allowed to tear around
the cardboard course at church fairs in order to
nut a new chasuble on the vicar, l don’t quite see
how the clergy can reasonably object to the Tote.
And until the lucky-bag, fish-pond, spinning-
jenny, and other games of chance are heard of no
more in ecclesiastical circles I don’t quite see how
Mr. Chadband can jump with a clear conscience
on the worldling who backs his fancy. But the
watery church-bazaar will pan out a still more in-
significant div. than usual if they really take away
the lotteries and hunt out the fortune-tellers, and
insist on the flower-girls washing the paint off
their lips—but I don’t think they will. Unless
the church has something tricky in Society ser-
pentining or an entirely new and startling array of
living pictures up its full bishop’s sleeves, I am
afraid the Reformation is still a long way ahead.
Apropos parsons—a very, very free-and-easy
Sydney cleric, who used to make life hideous for
the joily Jack Tars when in port, always hunting
them up to services of sacred song, tea-and-scone
struggles, and secular entertainments, at which
t'.ie rawest talent in town would rend the air
with shrill songs or breathless recitations —1 hear
that this giddy philanthropist has turned up at the
Cape. Sailormen are therefore advised to look
out for themselves on Africa’s coral strand, or he
may catch ’em a<pin with black vocalists and
cocoanut suppers this time.
The first concert of the 15th season of .Sydney
Liedertafel was a reviving incident in Sydney
Town Hall. Vice-royalty occupied the centre of
the wide half-circle of patronage —placed as usual
in the very teeth of the music—but 1 suppose by
this time they know enough to put wadding in
their ears. I was rather amused at the way the
patrons had arranged themselves —all the men on
the one side like a synagogue, though, to be sure,
the president’s charming wife, who re-appeared in
Society for the first time (in a chiffoiiey-looking
white gown and diamonds) after her long )>eriod
of mourning, was sitting next to His Excellency,
and so broke the line of black coats. Lady
Hampden wore a matronly turn-out and a feather
in her hair, and the three vice-regal damsels were
in heliotrope and white. The stage was gracefully
dressed with feathery verdure, and the immense
audience had a bow in its hair, generally speaking.
By the wav, a group of girls with bows in their
hinder locks were so arranged that the effect of
the said bows was exactly that of the Southern
Cross, looking down from the gallery. Is this
going to be a fatality in Australian decoration ?
The Liedertafel sang with all the ease of vocalists
who have been rested during the summer and are
conscious that their voices are as fresh as their
shirt-fronts. The long, heavy item, “ The Luck
of Kdenhall,” was, T suppose, above criticism, as
the score was given to the society, and one must
not look a gift horse in the mouth. At any rate,
it was a good ad. for the lung powers of the
Liedertafeilers, who were in inspiriting form all
through the concert, and, with the Liedertafel
orchestra, made the real successes of the neatly-
compiled programme. Miss Ilossow and Signor
de Beau puis were the extraneous talent lending
variety to the show. Miss Rossow (who is going
’Ome) wore a canary-colored frock, and sang her
songs in a high, clear, but somewhat nasal soprano
and got wild encores for each number. Du
Beaupuis, of the horse's fetlock, contributed piano
soli.

at Lawrence Campbell’s elocutionary and musical
