doesn't intend going 'Ome on her vo ce, which is a
rather hard and not very big contralto of ordinary
compass. Besides Lawrence Campbell’s versatile
recitations, which were the most popular items on
the programmes, there were organ and piano solos
by Miss Lilian Frost, songs by a nervous, throaty
vocal tyro, cud a lovely nocturne by Edgar cello
Strauss which atoned for all raw places observable
on the bill
The much -anticipated “ Djin Djin,” which broke
out at Sydney Lyceum last Saturday, proved a
success of satin and scenery set to airy, friendly
music, and seasoned with legs and low comedians.
It seems just the spectacle for the festive Easter
season, when the hairy individual who has g<me-
cn the-land-young-man comes to town for the
horse-show, and really needs a large, gorgeous
spendthrift entertainment like “Djin Djin ” to
plaster over the arid places in his mortgaged and
grass-grown soul. The dresses, i'i Ut Jolly Japan,
are very tine—all of them, except the golden ballet,
being from designs by Mr. \V. R. Barnes, of
Melbourne, who displays his maiden attempt at
frock-planning in these expensive garments, and
who, I hear, shortly goes ’Ome. The scenery
broke the artistic and gorgeous record, Gordon
getting the lion’s share of applause—though 1
liked a realistic bamboo grove (Goatcher) as well
as anything. It wasn’t gorgeous, of course, but
the long stems and the silvery foliage wore ad-
mirably expressed. One could almost hear it
rustling.
Flo Young, as Prince Eucalyptus (the gorge of
this paper rises at the bare notion of the possi-
bility of such a title), cut a fine figure in a blaok-
and-silver pot-up, with hair poudre and a
“ Sappho ” hat—though black-and-silver always
reminds one of coffin-plates. Her second suit of
cinnamon-brown cloth and green velvet with
round cap and Home Rule hose also suited her to
aT. Flora Graupner was handsomely done as
the Princess Iris, her chiefest efforts being white
satin painted with purple irises and a pmk-and-
white satin brocade combination, the colors melt-
ing into one another like eoooanut-ice; but tle
tiny Flora hasn’t enough “ shop front on which
to display such goods to the fullest advantage.
However, her smallness enhanced the manly
stature of Flo Young, who cuddles her in a man-
ner that many a male beginner might do worse
than imitate.
The dainty danseuse, Mary Weir, seemed rather
wasted as Dede, with only one hoppy dance to
do, but her chief robe of pink brocade and Bur-
gundy velvet was fetching, and she toddled like a
native. The Fairy Queen was the Melbourne ex-
amateur, Miss Orelli, who is probably the nrst
impossibly-blonde fairy to take to the stage in her
very own undyed hair. Miss Orelli’s warble is a
sweet, though not extraordinary, soprano, but she
was too unaccustomed, on Saturday, to turn it full
on at the tap. The ballet-dresses (I believe they
go by that name) were sportive flights of fancy,
especially the Snow ballet, with white Jack 1 rost
caps and muffs ; though I confess the I' loral ballet
was garish in places, especially a green girl out-
lined in scarlet, like lettuce garnished with toma-
toes. I liked the whirling Umbrella ballet, under
the dome of lanterns— it was quite a valuable
object-lesson to those people who manage to catch
your back-hair on their gamps, even when there
are only two of von in the street; and a crowd of
youngsters fluttering about looked like butterflies
in a garden.
One thing surprises me in the low, not to say
vulgar, comedian who dresses up as a woman— he
always omits to pad himself where his Bloomers
ought to widen out. Surely this is a great over-
sight on the part of one who leaves no stone un-
turned to make a realistic sketch. I notice,
by the way, that the man-woman of the stage
deteriorates in the flight of ages. The Widow
T wan key, if you remember, was gav and frisky,
yet you felt she had some rude notions of self-
respect; but the latest he-female loiters in public
places and is wholly objectionable. I am sorry
for this fall from grace on the part of the he-she-
comcdian, who doesn't seem half so funny in the
new edition.

at Sydney Town Hah, looks as if 'twould be the

keep away. A poultry supper- alas for the gross-

e\ en ng. One is not accustomed to such magnani-
mity between ball committees, who usually tight
tO r a finish. In the undyingly active secretary

Sydney sailing men mean to receive Gov.
Brassey with full nautical honors, and next
Saturday, April 11, every species of craft from
steam yachts to canvas dingies will be racing for
dear life up and down the harbour in honor of the
V ictonan Gov. A strong sub committee of well-
known yachting-men has the affair in hand, and
the Brassey regatta will practically end the sailing
season with a brilliancy equal to fireworks.
A new horror is observable in connection with
suburban mayoresses who are launching out into
otheial at-homes with free drinks and hand music
like the big mayoral receptions seen through the
wrong end of one s opera-glasses. A mayoress in
a W ostern suburb set the fashion last week. I
hear that the gnawmg fear of local munieipaldom
vas that there wouldn’t be enough people to eat
the retreshment, but, of course, the presentiment
uas groundless; the lady had quite a bumper
house, and not a bun left over for the parrot. But

The Wild-Celt Column.
The Colonial Mutual, which ranks after the
A.M.P. and Maoriland .State Life Department as the third
largest life assurance office in Australasia, has finished a
good year. The premium income for ’95 was £803,983,
and £89,703 interest on investments made the total in-
come 4393,6-6. The death and endowment claims were
£121,3-14, nearly £BO9O less than last year; and £130,206
was added to the funds, making the splendid total of
£1,923,835. The working expens s are £77,583, equal to
2.) 4 per cent, on premiums, or about 19|j per cent, on the
total income. Despite the bad times, therefore, the
Colonial Mutual has El2uo more income than it had last
year ; its expenses, which have been gradually diminish-
ing for a number of years, have again declined, and have
now, for the first time in the society’s history, got below
2o per cent, of the gross income ; and the amount saved
out of the year's revenue, after allowing for sums
written off, is about £4OOO more than it was in ’94.
The assets also look better than they did a year
or two ago. “ Government and other debentures ” re-
present £163,666, which is £lOO,OOO more than they were
at the beginning of 94, and, considering the general ten-
dency to run too much into the treacherous mortgage
business, this is a move in the right direction. “ Rents
and interest outstanding and accrued ” are only £17,705,
against £55,0u0 two years ago—which is another great
improvement, and if the society would divide up this
item, as many other offices are now doing, and show ex-
actly how much rent and interest are in arrear, and how
far they are in arrear, and also embody in the auditors’
certificate a plain statement tiiat all the arrears taken
credit for are in this item, and none of them, by
any chance, included among the mortgages, it would make
a further distinct improvement. Also, concerning a small
item, “preference shares, £12,710” (presumably bank
shares), it would be gratifying to know that these' shares
are taken at present selling-price, and not at nominal
values. The Colonial Mutual's balance-sheet is very satis-
factory in most respects, hut still, like most such docu-
ments, it might he a trifle more explicit.
Why do not the Australasian life offices follow
the example of, say, the New York Life, in the matter of
their assets? That office publishes a list of all bonds,
debentures, and shares held by the society, showing their
book-values and their present selling-price, and every
separate item of its freehold and leasehold property, with
the value set a ainst each item. Under these conditions
any seriously inflated estimates can hardly fail to attract
the attention of somebody. It also gives a list of its mort-
gages with the estimated present value of the properties
mortgaged as compared with the amount lent. The
policy of keeping strictly dark about details, followed by
the Australasian insurance societies, doesn’t pay in the
long run.
* * * *
The dreadful Real Estate Bank (Melbourne)
reports that the liabilities are c 1,229,333, or, leaving out
the shareholders’ capital, which is wholly gone, the amount
still owing to depositors and others' is £584,713. The
assets are reported as worth £23,636, and, considering the
pathetic smallness of the amount, one dismal liquidation
should he presently finished and the name of Munro’s in-
stitution be dropped into oblivion.
♦ » * *
The National Mutual Life Association has
£169,694 to divide as the profits of the last three years.
Of this amount £85,090 goes to policy-ho’ders, providing
reversionary bonuses to the extent of about £200,060;
£70,000 goes to the reserve for depreciation of securities,
and £14,694 is carried forward. The £70,000 reserve for
depreciation makes a big hole in the bonus, but the wis-
dom of this move is unquestionable. In these days the
securities have such a dismal tendency to fall in value,
that large reserves are absolutely necessaiy. It is a
question now between large bonuses and a fair chance
of ultimate burst or smaller bonuses and safety, and
the first object of every life office should be safety. And
with this object in view there is a fair chance that the
bonuses will have to get still smaller before any improve-
ment sets in.
» * * k
S.A. State Land Bank has started business by
iss ling at Adelaide £50,000 worth of 3.J per cent, bonds to
provide funds for loans, ful l her issues to follow when
more funds are required. This scheme is vastly more
business-like than that adopted in Maoriland. The M.L.
State Land Bank started by issuing a L 1,500,000 loan in
London, and as only about one-third of this amount has
been lent, the balance is costing the State in the mean-
time some 430,000 a year in interest to no purpose.
* * * *
The Farmers’ Co-operative Fire and Marine In-
surance Co. (M.L.), though only five years old, seems to
be doing well. Last year it scooped in £6217 in premiums
and £2898 interest on investments, and its losses were
only £756. The total profit was £43lo—a figure wh'ch
must make many a luckless co. feel bad with envy ; and,
alter paying 6 per cent, to shareholders, returning 10
per cent, of shareholders’ premiums and carrying £lOOO
to reserves (making £2675), it has still a small balance left.
* » » »
The United Australian Fire Insurance Co.
reports that its premium income for the year was £18,105
(about £5OO more than in ’O4), and the profit, after writing
off £893 for depi»ciation of securities, £2102. A 6 per
cent. div. absorbs £lBB4, and the b dance goes to profit
and loss, making it £2236. The United Australian has
been 16 years in existence, and hasn't even got a reserve-
fund started. Also, it has got a £27,000 mortgage on its
office property in Melbourne and Sydney—value of pro-
perty not stated, as it is lumped in with the “loans on
mortgage.” Its premium income is smaller than it was in
1887, and the amount received as interest on investments
is £699, whereas in 1887 it was £1570. Its investments
yield only 2 per cent, net on their alleged value, which is
a serious e lection on the alleged value, or on some-
th in g con in cted with them, and despite this state of things
the co. a vear ago paid away in dividend £5OO more than
its year’s receipts. The U. A. is a fine example of the hand-
to-mouth office which never seems to get any further
forward.

The Equitable Life Assurance Society of New
York furnishes in another column a statement of the
results for the past year. The figures show the steady
expansion of this colossal institution, whose business
keeps growing larger and larger through good times and
bad with unfailing regularity. It is announced that the
tontine policies maturing in '9O will show as good returns
as did those which fell due in ’95, which, considering the
fall in the rate of interest and the general depression, is
highly satisfactory. * , *
Last year the value of coal exported from New-
castle (N.S.W.) was only £678,000. In 1891 the exports
were worth £1,160,000.
In 1890 the butter imports of the United King-
dom increased by 493,000 cwt. over those of 1893, and
Lb ,ooOcwt. of this was supplied by Victoria. The rest of
the Australias, put together, only increased their exports
to Britain by 37,000 cwt.

Out of every eight fat men in Vic. Legislative
Council five are directors of one or more financial institu-
tions. Some of them are six-fold directors. Hence the
“° llnc 9 complaint voiced by J. William son at the City of
Melb. Bank enquiry, that “ no gentleman of standing and
principle ” will care to take office under the proposed
New Companies Act, which prohibits him from recklessly
operating on the firm’s till.
* * * «
The peculiarity of the City of Melbourne Bank
case, as well as that of the Federal Bank of Australia and
the Mercantile Bank, is that no criminality on the part
of the directors has been m im'/este l,” says Smellbourne
Arous. Truthfully speaking, the peculiarity is not in the
cases, but in the interpretation of the precious Victorian
law. Criminality is a relative term. Some countries
recognise the burial of a live widow with her dead hus-
band as a strictly legal proceeding. Had the three banks
aforesaid been born in London, instead of Melbourne, the
Arous might have viewed one or more of the directorates
in quite a different light.

In the New Chum and Morning Star, which
latter includes the Easter Gift, Mount Magnet, W.A. (says
Geraldton Express) has two properties that for over two
years have had their batteries continually running. The
New Chum, with a fair supply of stone, averages soz.
and over to the ton ; the Morning Star, with an im-
mense body of formation, which runs through the bat-
tery like sand, averages slightly under loz. to the ton.
Both these mines have declared enormous dividends com-
pared with the amount of labor employed. The dividends
have not been disclosed, but have been sliced up quietly
among the shareholders in buying machinery and other
mines.

ever saw that sand-dune damnation. “ Men are glad to

tinned-dog as home comforts.

* » • *
Stated that the Boulder—shares on their way
towards £8 at time of writing—could double its output
if it had the water and plant. With 2)0 men at work and
only crushing 209 to 309 tons fortnightly, the mine must
have an enormous accumulated tonnage at grass, or is
doing something extraordinary in the way of develop-
ment. Oti one hand it is said that the soz. and 6oz. stuff
is carefully dressed, and that the lode, beyond the shadow
of a doubt, is poorer below 150 ft. ; on the other, that the
ore is crushed as it is broken, and it is as rich at 209 ft. as
anywhere Anyhow, it is time that the public suspicion
on this point was removed by an official statement of
details. The Boulder is Lane’s magnificent mistake—it
can’t be forgotten that his original report of it was by no
means so glowing as those he made on other mines—the
Empress of Coolgardie to wit.
The Lake View lode is said to be as rich and
strong as ever 50ft. below the solid diorite.

The genera] opinion throughout Omeo district
(Vic.) is that Mines-Minister Foster will experience a dull,
sickening slump at next election. The pamphlet issued
from the Mines Department, at his instigation, cracking
up the Gippsland fi Ids, and Omeo in particular, is
described by the min rs as a very fine fairy tale, and has
deceived many. The men hold that the Minister's sympa-
thies are all with the leaseholders. At Dargo High Plains,
for instance, all the ground is pegged out, and only four
claims are working.

Shareholders in the Great Northern Extended,
Rutherglen (Vic.), stand on velvet. Dividends £129,000
£6 4s. on each £1 paid-up scrip (20,000). Record for field.
Chiltern Valley United is close up with £120,000 odd,
nearly all in the grip of “ Johnny ” Wallace, who had the
pluck to see the mine through its early troubles. The
G.N. lead averages 300 ft. to 350 ft. wide. Weekly yields,
from 120 oz. to 1300 z., with every chance of improvement
when the drives from the new shaft pick up the old run of
wash.

18in., and should it pick up in size and maintain its
quality the holders will have a straight “ go ” for a com-
petency.

and he was sorry the departmental regulations prevented
him from investing. Whether the mine was salted for
Parkes is a question Rogers, who is now doing his 18
months, can perhaps an ,wer.

Thus the Warden re Greenhill’s rush, 25 miles
from Georgetown, and three miles from the Gilbert. Three
hundred men are working the flat between two hills.
Specimens running from ioz. to 182 oz. gold are found.
One nugget of 150 oz. was brought in. From Dec. last
BDooz. have been obtained—mostly from three specimens.
No permanent water, and the creeks are almost dry.
The very latest. AQ. mines agency declared a
magnificent 39 per cent. div. some little time back, and,
after people in London had bought at £2, it was coolly
announced that the div. was payable in scrip in one of
the co’s mines at the co’s valuation.
A promising Tasmanian copper-mine, the Rio
Tinto, on the Savage River, is now under offer to an
English co. at a big figure. Now, Rio Tinto is the name
of one of the world’s best-known copper-mines—in Spain.
There seems very little originality in the Southern
Hemisphere.

* * * *
Editor Bulletin, — Re a recent par. in your
paper concerning jewellery-canvassers and the necessity
for their holding licenses as hawkers. A friend of mine
bought s ime jewellery from one of the fraternity, paying
10s. deposit. The principal repudiated the sale, and de-
manded the articles back, stating that the canvasser or
dealer had no instructions from him to sell anything. My
friend saw a lawyer, and was advised he would have to
return the articles, and could make no claim for what he
had paid. Now, if this canvasser had been licens3d, a
purchaser would have been protected to a great extent;
as it is, if a principal repudiates a sale, the buyer has no
claim on the sub-dealer.
* • * *
Recent huge defalcations by prominent solicitors
forcibly call attention to the absolute necessity of e:tab-
lishing the Maoriland Public Trustee system in all the
Australia*. Heartrending cases of destitution are con-
tinually being brought about by the reckless “ private
enterprise” plan, and the unfortunate objects of trusts
are at present quite helpless in the matter. They have
the choice of an investing solicitor who may be a gambler
on stock exchange or turf, or even a regular church-goer,
or of a bank which will probably go bung, or of a trustee
co. which may possibly depend on an uncalled capital—
uncallable when it is wanted. With a luilic Trustee
system, the defaulting trustee would be a luxury of the
past.

Reported that a <£2,000,000 German syndicate
is hustling round Northern Australia over a big stock
deal. Scheme—to treat stock-products on a large scale,
and plant freezing, hoi ing-down and fellmongery works
at convenient points, connected by light railways built on
the land-grab system if possible.
* * * * *
From time immemorial in N.S.W. a man sued
for a debt in one of the superior courts has only had to
file a plea of “never indebted” to delay the hearing of
the case for months. If he had to swear to the truth of
his plea when filing it, the fear of perjury-prosecution
wou.d deter him from entering a defence at all. A radical
and urgently-needed reform, which could be effected by a
three-line hill.

Labor-candidate Wallace Nelson’s nomina-
tion-paper was discarded by the Musgrave (Q.)
returning-officer because the date when that officer
notified the issue of the election-writ was inserted
instead of the date of the issue of the writ.
Brownlee’s paper, for the Herbert, was rejected
for a very similar reason. In Nelson’s case, the
returning-officer was specially asked if the paper
was technically in form, but refused to say. It is
quite time that common-sense, not to say fairness,
was imported into such matters. Why should a
candidate be disqualified by a political opponent
on the ground of a trivial irregularity which could
be set right by a stroke of the pen ?
The entrance-hall of the Equitable Society’s
Melbourne palace is considerably less iirqtosing
than the great “ well ” of the Sydney building,
which stands on a deeper piece of ground. In
Melbourne the E.l.S.’s hall has the elevators in
the background on either side, whereby the
gorgeous gallery effects are partly shut out from
the up-view. Otherwise this marble, granite and
steel structure is much the same as its Sydn y
relative, and would rank as the boss wonder of
Australian architecture if the other place hadn’t
been built. In the matter of fatalities to masons
employed upon the job it actually stands pro-
em inent.
Sweating in the Melb. bakery trade has
assumed alarming proportions- -unionist shops in
the suburbs are the exception. The sweating
doughy gets men to work 14 hours a day (longer
on Fridays) for as little as 12s. fid. a wee.k —los.
and meals being the height of munificence. The
Union wage is £2 55., and the proprietors who ob-
serve Union rules lump the Leg. Council, which
smothered the Anti-Sweating Act, and the-alleged
democrats, who mostly buy from the sweater, in a
comprehensive condemnation. The Unionist mas-
ters, in meeting assembled, agreed that £2 ss. is
little enough wage for a journeyman baker, but
that, if the sweater cannot be stifled, nothing less
than a 50 per cent, reduction in the pay of their
hands will enable them to continue to keep shop.
Gordon and Gotch’s “ Australian Handbook
for 18!)fi,” now to hand, is a standard book of refer-
ence, containing much information not to be
obtained elsewhere without great trouble and
research. Its maps of the various Australian pro-
vinces, of Maoriland, New Guinea, and Fiji; its
detailed descriptions of cities and towns through-
out the whole area of these countries ; and its
numerous statistical tables, are particularly valu-
able. The information, too, is remarkably accu-
rate and reasonably up-to-date, considering the
extent covered, and the new edition has undergone
very careful revision. This paper uses “ The Aus-
tralian Handbook” every week of its life, and
gladly recommends it.
The hailstones that rained on Melbourne,
the other Sunday, were said to be the biggest seen
there for 40 years. But they were puny missiles
compared with some oblong lumps of ice which
came down in the Lilydale district, 20 miles from
Melbourne, shortly before Xmas. The lumps in
question averaged two inches long, Hin. broad
and half-an-inch thick, and they rained hard
enough to cut pieces from the fur of a cat ( aught
in the storm. The writer will swear to this state-
ment, because he saw the humiliated cat slink
home after the bombirdment with sundry bare
spots upon the back and ribs.

AT PER DOZEN.
The most important point for the consideration
of all persons who desire to possess photographs
of those nearest and dearest to them is with regard
to permanence rather than price. No photograph
that will turn yellow, break out in spots, or vanish
into nothingness within a few weeks can have its
value expressed in figures. When delivered, it
may shine like the enamelled mystery on a soap-
box, but the seeds of disease are there, and must
come to the surface. This may be regarded as
one of the certainties of cheap photography at per
do; en.

CAMEO
Gov. Brassey’s yacht Sunbeam arrived in
Sydney harbor the other day, with the millionaire
Governor—six millions is said to be about the
figure—himself on board, also madame and various
appendages. Comparing the accounts published,
■this loyal paper prefers, on the whole, the achieve-
ment of the Telegraph scribe, who gives most
details about the bedrooms and the bathrooms. A
lot of people like to read about lords’ bedrooms
and bathrooms. Generally speaking, the bedrooms
on the Sunbeam lie “ along a white lincrusta-lined
passage, past the bright and spotless little engine-
room ”:
Here is Lord Brassey’s dressing-room, remarkable for
the ingenious manner in which space has been utilised.
Opposite is a bath-room, all enamelled red for photo-
graphic purposes. . . . Beyond is a roomy white cabin
with wide brass bedstead and multitudes of little
drawers. . . . Lord Richard Neville’s cabin is next,
notable for some very fine etchings. . . . Lady Bras-
sey’s boudoir is a charming little room on deck. . . .
From here a stairway leads down to Lord and Lady
Brassey’s room, exactly beneath, with its big square bed-
stead. . . . The floor lifts up, and discloses a bath,
completely equipped, beneath.
The great Australian daily which knows its busi-
ness always sends a specialist round to poke a
Governor’s mattresses with a wise air and an
umbrella, and to peer into his cupboards, and to
lift up his trap-doors, and to smell his tooth-
brush. The great heart of this young demo-
cracy demands these things.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Gov. Brassey charges for admission to his
yacht on Sundays when she is in Melbourne
THE SUNBEAM’S VISIT TO SYDNEY—REGARDED HISTORICALLY.
waters. He gives the takings to the charities, it
is true, but if charity covers a multitude of sins it
doesn’t abrogate the law, and The Bulletin calls
upon the pietists who have been so hot against,
charging for admission to theatres on the blessed
“ Sawbath ” to have a go at His Excellency.
Gov. Brassey was the other day invited to
visit a Castlemaine exhibition of exceeding great
turnips and oleaginous swine, and gratefully
accepted. Then along came an intimation from
Castlemaine Council that that body was on its
official uppers, and felt itself unequal financially
to the task of entertaining his Lordship as the
representative of Her Gracious should be enter-
tained ; wherefore it was hoped his Ex. would
repress his mad longing to visit Castlemaine till
the Council had saved up enough to buy bottled
beer and biscuits and cheese, also a new hat, and
to get its other shirt washed, and dust the town,
and fix things up generally. The Governor sorrow-
fully withdrew hvs acceptance of the invitation,
and the swelled melon, and the ram of ancient
lineage, also the prize poley-cow, are said to have
felt their loss keenly on missing the opportunity
of being informed by His Excellency that he
understood that their great joy on seeing him was
not for himself alone, but a manifestation of their
affection for their Gracious Queen and that dear
land, &c., &c.
* * * »
Gov. Brassey’s want of tact is proverbial.
The unjovial mariner rolled up to a Melbourne
school function the other week, without the
slightest knowledge of the name of the principal,
his host. Before troubling to enquire as to the
identity of the person who had invited him to
lunch and speechify, His Ex. grabbed the hand of
caterer Skinner, and engaged him in a long yarn.
He knew Skinner, anyway, in connection with
chicken and champagne, and was probably moved
to congratulate him upon the quantity of refresh-
ments present. After which H.E. was heard
enquiring, in a ponderous stage-whisper, for infor-
mation re the giver of the feast. The Baron is
“ bad form.”
* * * •
Premier Kingston’s unruly members aren’t
all in his Ministerial following. In referring to
his eternal dispute with the Adelaide hospital
management he so far unbridled his tongue as to
call one member of the lion, medical staff a “medi-
cal Jack the Ripper.” Consequence : Resignation
of the staff and many of the members of the
board of management—only appointed the other
day, after the Nurse Gordon riots.

at Melbourne University Commencement this
year (Mar. 21): to wit, a master’s degree in Arts,
and bachelors’ degrees in Arts (8), Surgery (5),
Science (1), Music (1).

Brassey’s faith in his own greatness is such
that he allowed his under-study. Chief Justice
Madden, to act a part in the Equitable Insurance
Society show at Melbourne last week. The Gover-
nor stumbled through a few opening remarks, and
finished up with a string of himperial platitudes,
spoken from memory with his eyes cast down in
the manner of one who is reading something from
a slate. Presently the Chief Justice took the
floor, head erect, cheerfully conscious of his Irish
fluency, and proceeded to extinguish the vice-
regal effort. Madden took up the rdle of advocate,
or chief canvasser, for the E. 1.5., in which, by the
way, he has a warm policy interest. It was sweet
to hear him “barrack,” whilst the clerical staff of
the institution stood around remarking that
Madden’s speech was worth a couple o’ ’undred
pounds to the business if it was worth a penny.

* * * *
The little coal-mining township of Brunner,
on the west coast of the south island of Maoriland,
has achieved the sad fame of Creswick and Bulli.
By a gas explosion in the Brunner colliery, on
March 2(i, about GO miners were entombed, and,
though rescue parties toiled night and day to re-
lease them, the lives of all were lost.
Premier Seddon has been down the shaft with
the rescue-party, and offered to “do a shift him-
self, if necessary.” As there was plenty of trained
help available, and stout Seddon isn’t any longer
in the condition of a trained man, it obviously
wasn’t necessary ; and, considering the imminence
of the M.L. general-election, the offer might just
as well have been omitted. However, these con-
siderations may not have presented themselves to
the Premier, in the excitement of his manly
enthusiasm.
Even over such a terrible disaster is trailed the
slime of “patronage.” Gov. Glasgow has “con-
sented to become the patron of the relief fund.”
The “ patron ” of a spontaneous national impulse
to help distressed widows and orphans ; the bait
for Mrs. Grundy’s advertising guineas ; grotesque
conjunction ! Bid those agonised women at the
pit-mouth cease wringing their despairing hands !
Hearts are thrilling with sympathy for them, eyes
are filling with tears, men are risking their lives in
the work of rescue—“under the patronage of Gov.
Glasgow ! ”
A sturdy, prolific lot were those Brunner miners.
the list of names you read “ ten
children,” “nine children,’ “seven children,”
“ five children ” —hardly ever “two children” or
“onechild.” One man had a family of 12, the
eldest only 16 ! “He had lost his wife some
months ago.”
Comparatively blessed is the woman whose
husband is killed with 50 or 60 other women’s
husbands in a mining disaster ; for, in that event,
she is sure to be more or less provided for. But
if her husband were killed alone or slowly
petered out ’ through disease aided by starvation
she herself could follow suit as far as the public is
concerned. Advertisement and sensation and
patronage” are the life of Charity.
In point of sales the recent Sydney Artists’
Exhibition was but a moderate success—all Aus-
tralian art exhibitions are, from that standpoint,
but moderately successful. Only a couple of dozen
pictures, and those mostly very low-priced ones,
were disposed of.

The other day, in the back-country, All
Cow, from China, married Mary Eliza Smith,
whereupon Mary Eliza’s justly-incensed brother
advertised in the marriage-column of the local
paper : “ At Billy-Billy, 20th March, Ah Cow to
Mary Eliza Smith. Aged 22 years.”

About half the ladies who followed
M'Millan in the appeal to Colonial Sec. Branker
to raise the age of consent in N.S.W. are natives
of Scotland, where, The Bulletin believes, it is
still the law that a girl can marry at 12 years of
age without her parents’ consent.
* * * *
The “age of consent ” being only 16 in
England, where girls mature late, why should it
be 18 in semi-tropical N.S. Wales, as proposed by
the Social Gimlet Brigade ?
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —You got home when you
Webstered dancing “a fleshly spree.” So, also,
are church picnics and the other social enjoyments
where the sexes play and paw and fool with each
other. Frequently carried beyond the moderation
of virility, this, too, becomes a sexual excess—
which is immorality. Dancing has, at least, the
merit of being graceful. Ponder this, ye saints !
JIM.
* * * *
Henniker Heaton, remarks Sydney D.T.’s
interesting London correspondent, has, through
his Parliamentary return of divorces in Great
Britain, Europe, and the colonies, ascertained that
N.S. Wales is the “divorciest” of British depen-
dencies. The number of divorces granted in 1894
in Great Britain was 230, whereas in N.S. Wales
391 divorces were granted in the same year. Yet
the population of England exceeded 30,000,000,
and the population of N.S. Wales was about one
million ! Now that people know that the marriage-
knot can be easily loosed in Botany Bay, it is
anticipated that the tide of emigration will flow
freely in that direction.
* * * *
According to St. James's Gazette, Hogan’s
Legitimation Bill is only intended to smooth the
way for colonial dignitaries who desire to be re-
ceived into the arms of British “sassiety.” Wo-
men, already legal wives in England, and children
already legitimate in England, are, it seems, boy-
cotted for their concubinage and bastardy, and
Hogan appears to think that his new law, which
doesn’t enact anything new, will remedy this. The
absurdity of the real aims of the “ Colonial party ’
is sufficiently indicated by this measure, agreed to,
in pig-in-poke fashion, by the Australian Premiers.

There’s a peace beneath her eyelid
Such as love hath never gladdened,
And she walks within the shadow
Of her loneliness, unsaddened ;
But the little children waiting
Up in Heaven, outvie each other
Calling : “Marry, marry, marry ;
For we want you for our mother.”
M.L.

The Sydney temperance demonstration the
other afternoon was a queer sight. Half-a-dozen
more or less seedy devil-dodgers, a few black-
dressed, winter-faced women, and some vacant-
looking prigs were the leaders. The balance was
composed of children mostly under the age of 14,
all certainly too young to know what they were
doing. One of the features was a man on a trolly
with a face painted a most inartistic red, who cer-
tainly comported himself as no respectable drunk
would think of doing.
* * » »
A Victorian native recently died in Sydney,
leaving directions and cash for shipping his body
to Melb. for burial. When the mate, in tallying,
called from his manifest, “One coffin and corpse,”
the crew struck, and the consignment had to be
shoved back on the Quay before they would resume
work. Later in the day a packing-case was taken
on, and the mate yelled “One specimen natural
history in case. ” A ghost now cackles at mid-
night in Melb. general cemetery as it tells the yarn
to other shrieking spectres.

Westralian native prisoners are confined
at Rottnest Island, off Fremantle. The island
also contains the vice-regal marine residence. The
nigs, lately got up a corroboree for Gov. Smith
and family’s entertainment. Afterwards his Ex.,
knowing their weakness for academic speechi-
fying, is reported to have addressed the darkies
who, as before said, are prisoners and cannot
escape, as follows :
I am aware that you are here undergoing punishment
for offences against the law. Your cheerful submission to
its consequences, your physical appearance, and your
good conduct speak in your favor, and testify to you-
gratitude for the care that is taken of you. I have been
much gratified by the, to me, strange exhibition I have
just witnessed, and thank you for the trouble you have
taken to get it up.
After which, tobacco was served by order of the
Gov., and the proceedings closed with three cheers
f° r H.E. and Lady Smith,

The presence of magnetism at “spirit”
circles, and the revelations of Rontgen’s photo-
graphy, are accepted in many spiritualistic coteries
as a probable natural explanation of many of the
phenomena of clairvoyance ; while speculative in-
dividuals are already suggesting x sound-vibra-
tions as explanatory of what is called “clair-
audience.”
“RUBBING IT IN.”
An ingenious Adelaide woman has written
the Yic. Government, offering to journey to Mel-
bourne, and cure the sweating evil in two years, on
a pretty little principle of her own. She proposes
to conduct a crusade amongst factory-girls, with a
view to converting them into seraphic servants,
cooks, nursegirls, and kitchon-rouseabouts. All
she asks is that mistresses will aid her by making
the life of the hired girl a dream of happiness— by
putting on two shifts of servants, instead of work-
ing one poor, bedraggled slavey all the way round
the clock, and by allowing them to feed in the
'lining-room, and, it is to be presumed, to be
nursed, each by her pet-particular policeman, in
the drawing-room, and by giving them the use of
the piano and a latch-key. The would-be crusader
concludes with the assurance that her husband
“ would have no objection to her being away from
him for a year or two,” by which time she expects
to have made cheap and cheerful domestics of the
sweater’s victims. It is a bright idea, and, for its
accomplishment, only needs the conversion of the
average girl and the average mistress. The Bul-
letin would prefer to convert the average sweater
—it would be a smaller contract.
* * * *
The N.S.W. farmer, in some districts, is
practically “chucking it up.” The Bulletin
knows one locality, within 70 miles of Sydney,
where the sole source of revenue for a very large
proportion of the farmers now
is road-mending. In the wild
and general desire to go road-
mending contract prices have
been cut down until the menders
have given up hoping for any-
thing for themselves, and only
expect to get enough out of the
work to buy horse-feed until the
grass grows again. Recently a
contract which occupied two
men and two horses for five
weeks was taken for £3 —six
shillings a week for each horse,
and the men thrown in. There
is a fixed annual grant for the
maintenance of that road, and it
goes so far now that the road is
better kept than it ever was
before —in fact, it is almost the
only local asset. The road-
mending population is doing no
agricultural work, for it takes it
and the horse all their time to
earn enough to keep the horse
alive. The rest of the population
is either away looking for road-
mending to do, or else arguing
with the storekeeper about a bag
of flour on credit. The only
solvent man in the parish is one who wisely lets
his selection rip and acts as gardener for a capitalist
six miles away. He is only home on Sundays, so
on the other days his wife goes forth and beats
out the chronic bush-fire single-handed, and per-
suades the horse (24 years old) to come out of the
waterhole ; and rescues the baby in between times
when it has gone into the bush to look for snakes,
or has sat down on an ant-bed.
* * * *
The eternal fitness of things. In a recent
Brisbane raffle one of the prizes was a pair of
carriage horses. It was won by a lighthouse-
keeper.
THE “BAD” MAN AND THE “GOOD.”
The bad man speaks immodestly of woman and
her son ;
The “good ’’man speaks immodestly of God and
Number One;
The “ bad ’’ man “legs-up” Number Two, and
( hangs the prior figure,
(( ff oc, d ” man “legs-up” Number One, and
hangs the other nigger ;
The “bad ” man moves by strength and width and
breadth of mind and heart;
The “ good ” man moves by want of these through-
out the upper part;
The “ bad ” man, placed ’twixt hazy “ Word ” and
Nature’s plain demands,
Is forced to follow one of these—the one he under-
stands.
The “good” man simply follows nought, but keeps
them both in view ;
He screams at “ Bill’s ” neglect of one while he
neglects the two.

“I am an anchorite, and the world is mv
cell.” J
This may be an explanation of Dr. Fiaschi’s
departure: In Sydney there are two Italian
factions, one ultra-Conservative, the other ultra-
Demooratic, between whom there has long been a
deadly feud. The doctor was amongst the
former. Ihe Conservatives, on hearing of the
defeat of the Italians in Abyssinia, held a meet-
ing, and passed a resolution that “the national
honor should be vindicated ” by continuing the
tight. Ihe ultra-Democrats met, and while pass-
ing a motion condemnatory of the war, seized the
occasion to scoff at the patriots, and to sneer
at their violent desire to send other men to
continue the fighting, while they urged them on
from a safe distance. Fiaschi was always a sensi-
tive man—good surgeons, it is said, generally are—
and the gibes of his old-time foes goaded him to
vindicate himself from their aspersions in the only
possible way. So away he goes to the war, while
many of those here whose sufferings he alleviated
—among others, the writer of this— send a little
prayer that so brave and skilful a man may not be
a victim to Quixotic ardor.
The United Type-Writer and Supplies Co.
of London and New York, has introduced into
Australia the Densmore, the Standard Shift Kev
Machine, The World’s Greatest Type-Writer.”
Ihe Densmore family, its inventors, having been
connected, m a with type-writers since
these began and understanding more about them
than the rest of the Universe put together, have
crystallised their knowledge into the Densmore
The machine was adopted by the United States
War Dept, after competitive tests , and last year the
U.S. Govt, took on 300. The machine’s points
over others are its noiselessness; its very soft
touch; its superior paper feed; its moveable
envelope guides ; its instantly interchangeable
platens; its 84 characters. Being its own best
advertisement imitators are upon its track, but are
lost in the race for prestige. It is the easiest type!
writer yet put upon this market; and the above
co. with the long and noble name will give trials
and explanations free at 114 Pitt-street Sydney *

“ Djin-Djin ” opened in a burst of glory
at the Lyceum on Saturday, and asserted itself as
the most dazzlesome pantomime yet seen in
Sydney. This much may be said without fear of
contradiction, except from evil-disposed persons.
The money spent on more than one single scene
would have kept the staff of any ordinary paper
unsober for three weeks, and the Firm gets good
value for its expenditure, too. In some places it
has got the local coloring in strong, and a pleasant
flavor of Japan is wafted across the footlights ; at
other times it isn’t, but whether the scenery is
appropriate, or whether it is merely heaved in re-
gardless of time or place or circumstance, it is all
gorgeous. The earthquake is the most complete
thing that space could allow of. The big stage is
choke-full of old Japanese city just before the
catastrophe, and then the catastrophe comes along
with a great heave, and the city is left piled
several feet high on the stage. The earthquake,
in short, wrecks everything in sight, and no con-
vulsion could do more than that. The frozen forest,
and the Hall of the Thousand Storks, and the
Golden City, and the electric effects, and all the
rest of the magnificence, must be taken as read.
And with it all, if any one went away by himself
and read the book of the play in cold blood, he
would discover that “ Djin-Djin ” is a very ordi-
nary panto., with as little plot and literary merit
as the average ; there is one square inch of origi-
nal literature set in one square mile of expensive
frame and variety show. Pantomime in general
is spectacular drama gone out of its mind, only
“Djin Djin’’has gone out of its mind more ex-
pensively than the average.
* * * *
As regards the characters in the outbreak
proper/Florence Young is a fine, upstanding
sailor youth, and looks quite real in that male
garment which is best not mentioned. Flo. Graup-
ner is a good Jap. maid, and little Ivy Scott, if
possible, a still better one ; and Elton is reminis-
cent of the Insect and many other things. Also,
Edward Farley lifts up his splendid bass voice in
cavernous places as the Bogie Man, and a lot
of other capable people at large salaried come
and go without having time to leave a
decent-sized footprint on the show. Among
the people who rage on the outskirts of the
production, yet hardly belong to it, Coleman
is distinctly the best. As the usual objectionable
man in woman’s clothes he doesn’t amount to much,
but when he lets himself loose in his own proper
person he is a wildly hilarious scarecrow, and
makes more fun out of less material than any other
man in the show. This paper also desires to say
something fatherly and benevolent about several
of the crowd of child actresses, notably Carrie
Moore, Ida Ingersoll, and Isabel Hendley, who
may all consider themselves patted on the head
accordingly. Coleman, in his capacity as the
above-mentioned female, may consider himself
pounded on the head, which is a wholly different
thing.
** * *
Sydney Royal, Criterion, Her Majesty’s,
and the luckless Music Hall are all closed at time
of writing, but a great and general re-opening is
announced for Saturday. On that memorable
evening Bland Holt puts up his Highland-kilt
drama, “One of the Best,” at the Royal, and a
number of well-known people, whose faces are
sufficiently familiar, but whose knees the public
is not acquainted with, will exhibit these parts of
their anatomy for a change. The Bulletin’s
regret that Miss Elizabeth Watson doesn’t also
appear in kilts remains unabated, and it anxiously
suggests that perhaps some arrangement could be
made. On the same night, “ Called to Arms ”is
to blaze forth at Her Majesty’s. During last week
certain violent legal proceedings have been going
on, Bland Holt seeking to get an injunction, or
something, against the firm at H.M.’s on a ques-
tion of copyright, and it is understood that, if
things go wrong with the latter, they will fire the
Maxim gun intended for use in the battle-scene
promiscuously into the Full Court. Brough and
Boucicault also open their farewell season at the
Cri. on Saturday, with two nights of the “Pick-
pocket,” one of “ Rebellious Susan,” one of
“Dandy Dick,” and two of “Mrs. Tanqueray,”
to begin with. Then on Saturday (April 11), it is
proposed to stage “ The Importance of Being
Earnest,” written by a gentleman who is now
under a cloud and living on a light diet.
** * *
By way of further celebrating the hot-
cross-bun season, Harry Rickards and Alice
Leamar re-appear at Sydney Tivoli on Saturday,
and some other new people will be turned on at
the meter. At the Opera House “Trill-Bee,” a
burlesque on the heroine with the much-drivelled-
about feet, is to be staged, with Charles Cogill as
Svengali. Also, the Music Hall proposes to resume
its fitful existence, this time as a variety show.
In its dramatic capacity it died after a brief
struggle with “ East Lynne,” and thereby left a
great void. During its brief career it cast a com-
pletely new light on two dramas, and if fortune
had been more favorable it might have succeeded
in making “ Hamlet ” look so like “ Robbery
Under Arms,” and both of them so much like a
bush dog-fight, that no one would have known the
difference. The Music Hall, in this paper’s
opinion, should have been better supported.
** * #
Boucicault makes his valedictory Mel-
bourne appearance this evening (Thursday) dis-
guised in an Eton jacket. “ The Magistrate ” has
been selected for the farewell of the B. and B.
company, in order that a regretful audience may
be laughed out of any foolish determination to
drop a silent tear.
* * * »
Boucicault’s benefit speech at Melb. Prin-
cess’s last Saturday was disappointing. The actor,
who was obviously striving to make it appear im-
Eromptu, put no warmth into his words. Also,
is sidelong glance at Chief Justice Madden and
party, in the managerial box, when he attributed
the vast dimensions of his enthusiastic recep-
tion to the “ sympathy and loving kind-
ness of many personal friends,” was rather
distasteful to the stalls and amphitheatre.
Sympathy and loving kindness have very
little to do with the success of Saturday
night benefit performances. A popular bill, “ The
Amazons,” was offered to the public on this occa-
sion, and the public rolled up more freely than
they would have come under ordinary circum-
stances, mainly for the sake of hearing themselves
cheer more than usual in honor of a popular artist
and manager who was appearing for the last time
in his most humorous character. This was to be
a memorable occasion, with a speech added to the
programme. Therefore it appealed to the large
number of playgoers who like to be present when
something special is happening. Sympathy and
loving kindness, forsooth ! Boucicault and partner
Brough, when burnt out of the Bijou some
years ago, tried to repair the effects of their serious
calamity by resuming business immediately at the
Hibernian Hall. The new premises—out-of-the-
way and unsuitable for stage shows —put personal
friendship to a fair test, and found it quite
unequal to the strain.
** * *
On Easter Saturday Hie Firm’s new com-
pany from ’Murka, supplemented by evergreen
Harwood and a few other local recruits, will intro-
duce “ Trilby ”at the Princess’s. Everybody
seems to have either read Du Maurier’s hovel or
heard about its heroine’s feet, and the rush for
tickets is an assurance that “ Trilby ’’ will boom.
-& # # #
Melbourne Royal, after being submitted
to a scrubbing-out, will resume business on Satur-
day under C. B. Westmacott’s management. The
opening piece is “ The Enemy’s Camp,” and the
same people who lately earned a large amount of
respect for this gaudy spectacle at Sydney Royal
will give it another chance on the larger stage.
* * * * **
At the Opera House the course of true
variety business runs smooth. Charles Godfrey,
just now, chants praises to the Seventh Royal
Fusileers, and gets through three or four other
songs in particularly good style. Changes in
the second rank of the company are coming at
Easter.
** * *
Dan Barry’s cannibal feast is such an awful
attraction at Melbourne Alexandra that the
announcement of another lively horror for Satur-
day next seems uncalled-for. The gallery-boys
would gladly have listened to the drip, drip, drip
of blood from the Moocher’s heart until further
notice. Dora Mostyn imploring Gabbett to
appease his appetite with the stale fragments of
Moooher before starting to chop her (Dora) into
nice fresh joints, is a greater favorite than ever.
Herscreams, when Gabbett fondles his gory axe and
devours her lungs with his great goggle eyes, are
delightfully impressive. “The Sad and Solemn
Spectacle of a Quadruple Execution,” likewise a
“desperate sword combat between female con-
victs,” add to the well-deserved popularity of
“His Natural Life,” and, for these and other
reasons too numerous to specify, The Bulletin
recommends the Alexandra management not to
close Gabbett’s butchery establishment until after
Lent.
* * * -*
A sacred concert, decorated with living
pictures, is to be given at Melb. Exhibition Build-
ing on Good Friday. The Town Hall is engaged
by the Philharmonic Society, which threatens to
perform Gounod’s “ Redemption ” on a large
amateur scale.
At Poverty Point .
The fact that Brough is now taking music-lessons from
Benno Scherek, and is polishing up his vocal notes, points
strongly in the direction of his return to comic opera. In
the event of the B. and B. Co. dissolving, Titheradge, it is
thought, might retire awhile to his flower-garden at
Moonee Ponds. A dozen years’ mumming at a big salary
has enabled the steady Tith. to put by a few shekels. But
he should still have a long stage-career ahead. Look at
all the “ old ” parts he has to play !
The doll-like Ethel Haydon and her dog “ Smudgy ” fill
a page and a-half of a recent To-day with a drivelling
interview. The fair Ethel denounces “bikes” as un-
womanly ; and spiteful Melb. women will smile when they
remember the contour of the young lady’s legs as they
once appeared in Princess pantomime tights.
Mr. VV. T. Best, the organist, is reported to be hopelessly
ill at Liverpool, Eng. Mr. Best made many friends in
Australia, and was the only distinguished visitor to this
country who ever had the pluck (or acumen) to order two
whiskies-and-soda at once. The blackest aspect of Aus-
tralia, the eminent organist always said, was the slowness
of the hotel waiters.
Ada Crossle.v, writing on Jan. 30, already had 60
engagements booked for the coming season, and no longer
finds the British climate trying. Handsome is as hand-
some does.
The Argus critic charges Brough (in his character of
the farcical insurance-agent in “ Niobe ”) with “ playing
to the gallery more sedulously than is permissible even in
so farcical a production as ‘Niobe.’” If the “gallery”
that attends a 8.-B. performance were the brainless
groundlings that the pawky one suggests, Brough and his
friends would have an excuse, almost a duty, to please
them —for it is the “ gallery ” that has given this splendid
band of comedians the only* consistent support they have
received in Melbourne. But the “gallery” is not the in-
ferior part of the audience —the “ gallery ” is cosmopolitan
and Bohemian, and throngs to all kinds of clever produc-
tions-the stalls and circle are only rei ' l , l V r "
thusiasm by leg-shows and operatic tnviahties. T h e time
is past when literal “ playing to the galleiy
garded as an offence. It is the gal ery that is thefinaland
just arbiter for the stage. The tact that Brou„I v D
the gallery in “ Niobe ” is a facer to the cntio.

A stirring melodrama was recently played, the leading
parts being taken by a recently-married actress, an actor,
and the lady’s husband. The epilogue will be performed
in the divorce-court. Moral: Never let 3*our servant into
the secret of your amours.

duction of ‘ The Land of the Moa.’ ” A pleasant way of

Minnie Palmer will become, on May 15, Duchess
d’Estella.
Pastor Walters, unable to trade off his “Joseph,” is
returning to Australia.
The “ Trilby ” madness has reached Melbourne. “ Her
face was marked with a few of the sweetest little Trilbies
imaginable.” Trilby was freckled.
The late General Ponsonby was very polite, and on
every occasion when a company performed before Queen
Victoria, sent next day a wire inquiring after the artists’
health. The form was stereotyped and did for everybody.
Once, the proprietor of a troupe of performing geese was
commanded to Court for the entertainment of Her
Majesty’s grandchildren. Next day, the impresario
received the following message “ Her Majesty would be
delighted to know if the members of your troupe are well,
and if they have had an agreeable journey?”
Tommy Hudson,,who is closing his season in India prior
to returning to Australia, has beaten all past records this
trip. Mrs. Hudson goes to England in July, and Tommy,
for about the tenth time, threatens to retire. The Her-
mans have gone too; Harry Shine and the Bovis Bros,
follow.
Adelaide is promised a season of comic opera at Easter,
to be followed by “ Djin-Djin.” The announcement
nearly killed half the population. It is firmly believed
that the comic opera co. will turn out to be a troupe of
performing bears, after all.
Apropos the Watkins v. Marjoribanks breach of promise
of marriage, thus an English writer : “So far as one
knows, not a single marriage of the sort, except Lord
Orkney’s, has turned out satisfactorily. The Gaiety girl of
to-day is far from being (as ’twas customary to depict her
10 years ago) the victim of the aristocracy. She knows
the commercial value of virtue, and is as carefully
chaperoned as the well-shepherded Belgravian damsel.
She may call men by their Christian names, and she will
take all the jewellery she can ; but compromise herself,
never. The compromising is all on the masculine side.”
Decima Moore was on 20th Feb. married to the lengthy
Cecil Hope at Holy Trinity Church, Sloane-street, London.
Australia is woefully weak in actresses capable of doing
anything considerable with a big part, such as Fedora.
Mrs. Brough can, perhaps, play it rather more effectively
than Maud Williamson, whose throat complaint handicaps
her. And after Miss Williamson ?
Rumoured that “ Bob ” Brough will follow partner
Boucicault to England in about a year’s time. If true,
there is a poor look-out for serious drama and comedy in
Australia.
What a year of ’Murkan accent this will be in Austra-
lian theatres. Think of it: (1) “Trilby,” all ’Murkan;
(2) Potter-Bellew, half ’Murkan; (3) Hoyt’s “ Trip-to-
Chinatown” Co., all ’Murkan ; (4) Nat. Goodwin’s Co., all
’Murkan.
“ As You Like It” is to be the Potter-Bellew opening
piece at Sydney Lyceum. The pair will bring one or two
’Murkan players for leading parts, and the rest of the co.
will be drawn from local sources.
Veteran Harwood and Cyril Keightley are the only
local mummers who will figure in “ the firm’s ” “ Trilby.”
Harwood plays the serio-comedy part of the Rev. Thomas
Bagot. Phil. Goatcher has finished the scenery.
Out of the 100 plays produced in the ten vears of the
B. & B. partnership, Boucicault places the tollowing in
the order of the greatest pay-box successes “ Niobe ”
“ The Amazons,” “ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” “Dr
Bill,” “Caste,” “Dandy Dick,” and “The Village Priest.”
He regards “ Caste as the best all-round performance of
the co., “ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” as the best literary production
“ Diplomacy ” as the best acting play, and “ Mrs. Tan-
qimray” and “Fedora” as producing the finest individual
parts.
Mark Twain’s Indian tour, which R. S. Smythe intended
should last only five weeks, was prolonged because
Clemens, for the sake of his book, wanted to see the prin-
cipal historic cities, and because it was thought that the
Transvaal troubles might interfere with the South African
tour. The leading residents of Johannesburg are in "-aol •
and the Boers, who are not a humorous race mb-ht refuse
to allow them out even to hear Mark Twain. °
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Lord Sheffield is a curious, not to say eccentrio
character. During several years he shut up his beautiful
cricket-ground in Sussex because after the matches the
lads and lasses evinced a fondness for sundry quiet nook*
wherein to while away the hours of gloaming—a thing
which the “ bachelor earl ” could not tolerate. Now he is
offended with good-natured Stoddart because Stoddy,
instead of writing thanks to his lordship for an invitation
to meet the Rest of England at Sheffield Park, was con-
tent with a cabled reply. To further mark his disap-
proval, the earl will, it is said, not have any of Stoddart’3
team in the eleven against whom the Australians are to
open their campaign.
* « * •
Lyons and Ferris tip young Hill for top average;
Gregory fancies Graham, and Graham plumps for his pal,’
Gregorv ; while the public go nap on Iredale.
* * * *
Kelly and Johns have, it is said, to halve one
man’s share of the Australian Eleven profits—if there are
any.
* * * *
Harry lias gone to England in order, it is under-
stood, to get on as a professional with some English club.
Rather late—3B years—for a cricketer to make a new
beginning.
* * * *
In England last season, Surrey Richardson took
290 wickets for 4170 runs—average 14.37, and the editor of
Lillywhite’s Annual whoops that “ it is the best so far in
the history of first-class cricket,” forgetting that Charley
Turner, during his first English season (1888), took 314
wickets for 3492 runs—average 11*38. More wickets, less
runs, and 2 runs better average.
* * * *
Clem. Hill had to resign his billet in S.A. rail-
ways before departing for England, as he could not get
leave. Geo. Giffen, who is in the Post-office, always re-
ceives 12 months’ leave without pay.
* * * *
George Giffen’s sciatica is by no means to be
laughed over. He was warned about it months ago by an
Adelaide doctor, and underwent a course of massage
at the beginning of the season. He was' lately advised
that a more severe attack may follow at any time, but his
presence in the Australian team means “gate,” and the
advisability of his retirement was not even considered.
* * * *
When Martin had completed his series of “ex-
citing struggles ” with Parsons last Saturday, a harmless
admirer came behind him and slapped him on the back.
Whereupon the Yankee, thinking the friendly blow was
administered in a spirit of derision, smacked his admirer
across the nose.
* * * *
The question that naturally arises from the
Martin-Parsons match is : How much start could the
winner give the loser in three more races over the same
distances ? He certainly had something up his sleeve—
not much, but enough to swear by.
* » * *
Cheltenham (Vic.), a hitherto peaceful marine
village, about a dozen miles from Melbourne, the moods
of whose cliffs on a day, half-blue, half-grey, would cap-
ture the soul of any artist, has, of late, been invaded by
battalions of pushful cyclists. It has got lev el with them
by legally proclaiming a rough and unbikable bit of road
just outside the township to be footpath, and fining
wheelmen (beginning with University professor Marshall
Hall) who venture to cross it on wheels.
• » * *
The cyclist who is not married but something
similar is quite a drug in the Australian athletic market
just now. Boss Martin is one of the very few notable
exceptions.
* * » *
The racing cyclists of Vic. have had distributed
among them at metropolitan meetings alone no less than
£lB5O this season. In all, the Vic. season’s cash-prizes to
date total at least £2500.
* * * *
Cyclist Martin has, so far, netted £355 since his
arrival in Australia. His Italian season, concluded last
August, panned out £7OO, and, as the iron-jawed Irish-
American cuts down ex’s to the bone—travels third-class
by boat and second by train everywhere—he should, by
this time, have a nice little capital wherewith to start
the saloon which it is his ambition to run on returning
to the States.
* * * *
Two cyclists who married at Young the other
day spent the honeymoon on wheels. A veracious country
chronicler says that “ after making due allowance for
stoppages they reached Dubbo in good time.”
* * * *
The forthcoming benefit to Megson and Lewis is
a liberal move by the N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League, and
should pan out well. The cyclists named, having secured
the needful, will go to England on a racing trip. Both
are first-class riders who, for long past, have not been
seen at their best. In England they will doubtless be
retained by some firm as a machine advt., which should
make their living sure ; and provided neither gentleman
falls over-seriously in love, or gets married a la Ziin., they
should bring home a fair bagful of English oats.
* * * *
Wallace has not yet won a race at Rand wick.
Last year he was downed by both Bob Ray and Georgie.
* * * »
Thus “ Scrip ” :—Doncaster mile, the chief
handicap tussle at Randwick to-day (Saturday), should
furnish a pleasant guessing competition. At time of
writing things bear a somewhat mixed aspect owing to
absence of acceptances, but where so many well-backed
neddies are in evidence, it will probably prove a case of
“But it on, and let us pray.” A much-of-a-muc ne s
characterises the leading lights, but on past form mid
breeding the Hotchkiss horse, True Blue, appears to be as
good as any. Albicore, if started, may show a true run,
but weight should anchor unlucky Gourallie. On Spring
efforts, if Te Whiti is M‘Sweeney’s best, his 6.12 ought to
see him through. Vagabond is the usual late rumor, but
the only other animal likely to cause consternation is
Laura, who has cost Oxenham a lot of money lately.
Knocking the crowd down to a trio, the writer goes for
Te Whiti, True Blue and Laura.
The public have put their little bank on Wallace
for Sydney Cup, and he may strike Ikey Mo’s note-chest,
but, as the weight has only once previously been carried
home by a three-year-old, the colt is just as likely to
cause blighted hopes. The Harvester is said to be not
quite “ fit,” but The Trier is if he doesn’t shirk the end-
up. Carmody may have Glenloth-luck with Music, who
has a pull in the weights, but so has Chesterman. It
doesn t generally matter, though, what rogues have to
carry. All the same, Chesterman may run the better.
Ihe writer has no special fancy for Toreador, who is a
certain starter. So is Damien, but a guess can only be
made at the rest, though perhaps Laura may emulate
her dam, Cerise and Blue. She can stay well enough at
a l>. v ' ra te. Wallace, Laura, and The Trier, therefore, is an
off-hand selection early* in the week.
A. J.C. St. Leger looks a paper moral for Wal-
lace, whose jock, will have his eyes skinned against a
repetition of the Flemington Leger hanky-panky. If the
colt loses this race, people will begin to think he was born
on a Friday.
Coil’s alleged return to form may put a different
complexion on Newhaven’s A.J.C. Champagne Stakes
prospects. The pair are the champions of their respective
provinces, and if the much-discussed Huret is saddled the
go should swarm with interest.

MAD!
He glowers within a padded cell,
A gentle youth, and fair to see,
And yet, from what the warders tell,
How very mad the lad must be.
He is a cricketer of might—
How past belief the tale must seem—
Who doesn’t think he’d any right
To go ’Ome with the Austral team.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A motto for Australia : “Off color.”
Premier Nelson’s column— with a Kanakr
on top.
One of the strongest men working against
Premier Kingston in the big political fight just
commencing is his legal partner, George Ash.
The State tobacco-monopoly idea is taking
firm hold in Victoria, where it will yet bean estab
lished fact and act as precursor of a State grog-
monopoly. 6 8
The S.A. general elections take place on
Saturday, April 25. Already 110 candidates are
definitely out for 54 seats. In ’93 the number who
went to the poll was 128.
Last session of M.L. Parliament a Bill
authorising a bonus on the first 10,000 tons of
beet sugar narrowly escaped being passed. It is
to be re-introduced next session, with every pro-
spect of success. *
No Government can last in Victoria which
will not fight to the bitter end for the rejected
Shopsand Factories, Companies’, and Employers’
Liability Bills. Premier Turner knows this, and
is prepared to do battle.
All the R.C. candidates at Q. elections
had a satisfactory answer for “ Are you prepared
to maintain the present system of State educa-
tion V “Are you prepared to vote for subsidising
R. C. schools ? ’ was what knocked them out.
Last year’s death-rates of Australasian
capitals : Brisbane, 11'63 per 1000 ; Wellington,
12-50 ; Sydney, 13-19; Adelaide, 14’86; Melbourne,
15-97; Hobart, 16T1 ; Perth, 26-06 ! What a
graveyard is the stinkful city of the Six Families!
The Canadian Government has offered
a regiment of cavalry to assist the Upper Nile ex-
pedition. But you don’t hear the tramp of the
Soudan ’bus-horses in Sydney streets to-day.
Exp. docct. Donkeys are dear at =£350,000 apiece.
Mr. Hector Lamond, who contests Cowra
(N.S.W.) electorate as a Labor-candidate, stood
last election as the accredited Freetrade nominee.
Mr. Lamond is a printer, having served his time
with the Carcoar Chronicle, of which he is now
lessee.
Maoriland, with 11 months of its financial
year gone, sees its way to a surplus of =£150,000.
And, meanwhile, one-half the daily papers are
busy pointing out that Maoriland is-ruined by
Protection and the other half that it is utterly
destroyed by Socialistic legislation.
From London Daily Telegraph leader :
“ England is so united that she need not be in the
least disconcerted even though two hemispheres at
once menace the Queen of Civilisation, the Ran-
somer of the Slave, the Mother of Free Govern-
ments, and the Mistress of the Seas.” One lie,
one hypocrisy, one self-delusion, and one empty
boast.

Dr. Hodgson’s (N.S.W.) scheme of com-
pulsory voting at parliamentary elections is not
* s ,, old \ In 1646 Virginia fined non-
voters 1001 b. tobacco.” In 1636 Plymouth im-
posed a money fine ; and many other places before
and since have tried the expedient.

SOME SMELLBOURNE SIGHTS.
Two captive balloons. Comer of Collins and Swanston-streets.
The Turner Government is understood to
be hunting up democratic candidates to run against
the eleven Tory obstructionists who retire from
Vic. Council next August. There has been no
outward blaze of fury and large red adjectives so
far, but the general impression is that the Turner-
Isaacs-Peacock combination means to make a
bitter struggle to eject eleven of the forty-eight
dreadful fossils when election-time arrives.
PREMIER REID DECLARES WAR AGAINST THE RABBITS.
THE WAR MAY END THUS WISE—
OR THUS.
Speaking at Wellington, John Haynes
“ defended the N.S.W. land-tax as the first signal
victory of the country over the metropolis, and as
the first achievement in the programme of decen-
tralisation. He condemned the income-tax, and
said that he meant to give Premier Reid no
quarter on the subject. It was as unjust a tax on
industry and enterprise as protection was, and no
true liberal could defend it.” A straw showing
the direction of the Foreign-trade wind.

The S.A. Anglican Church-party
is industriously whipping up woman
voters, in view of the poll to be taken
in April re religious teaching in State
schools and the question of capitation
grants to private denominationa
schools. The dissenting churche
strongly oppose, so that the presen
democratic system seems to be safe
for the present, anyhow.
Ex-rail way-chairman Speight’s
supporters are now wondering if, afte
all, the Argus advocacy of his claims
has improved his chances of reinstate-
ment. One day the railway men’s ido
is lifted by the Tory organ on to a high
pedestal, and then left severely alone
for a week or two. On the other hand,
the attacks of the AGEhave been con-
sistent and bitter, to the terror oi
friendly-disposed politicians. With
these facts and the Ministry’s openly-
avowed antagonism, Speight’s hopes
are now below zero.
Old James Service, M.L.C.,
alleges that if the Turner Govern-
ment’s Companies Bill (now residing
in the waste-paper basket) had been
in force in ’93 every director of the
reconstructed banks would have been
ruined. Considering that the bill
would merely have made those direc-
tors personally liable for false or mis-
leading statements issued by them,
this would indicate that they were a
very misleading crowd, to say nothing
worse. As matters stood, however, it
was the public that was ruined instead
of the directors, and a good part of
the public was ruined by old James’s
own bank while old James himself is
still peacefully afloat. Under these
circumstances it would have been a
good deal more decent if Mr. Service
had left the subject alone. The spec-
tacle of an aged senator barracking
for a system under which he has kept
up while many of the people who be-
lieved in him and his system were
ruined is hardly respectable.
Attorney-General Byrnes was sure that
the blessing of God was with his crowd at North
Brisbane election ; and Macdonald Paterson,
addressing his little coterie after the poll, said
“he knew they had been actuated by tne purest
of motives and aided by the help of God. ” Fancy
invoking the presidency of the Deity for a contest
between Bulcock and Yellow Pup ! After all,
your true religionist is the only real blasphemer.
The Bulletin suggests that there shouM
be published quarterly, in the N.S.W. Govern-
ment Gazette, a list showing the names of all
persons imprisoned for debt in the various gaols of
the province ; the amount each prisoner is in gaol
for, giving the actual debt and the law-costs separ-
ately ; how long he (or she) has been in on this
occasion, and, if possible, how long he (or she) has

The report of the Employers’ Union (Vie.)
significantly states: —
The Employers’ Liability Bill was introduced in the early
part of the late session of Parliament, and the Executive
at once took steps with kindred associations to prevent
the measure becoming law. . . The Bill did not reach
the second reading*.
Minister Sydney Smith, of N.S.W. Mines
and Agriculture, is busily contemplating the early
production of sonic half-dozen different pamphlets
on tobacco, silk, and other commodities which fall
within his strikingly heterogeneous command,
oome applicants for the writing-up of the works
sent in, as vouchers of their ability, selections from
their printed verses.
William Copley, the S.A. politician who
has gone over to the Ass, and now opposes pay-
ment of members, thus spoke in S.A. Assembly in
August, 1887:
I maintain that payment of members will sound the
death knell of monopoly and Conservatism in the Council
—(Which it has done.—Ed. B.). . . Payment of mem-
bers is the only means by which the principles of the
Constitution can receive full effect. . . Payment of
members will, by widening the area from which'members
could be chosen, very much improve the legislation of the
future. /
Thomas Joseph Byrnes, Attorney-General
of Kanakaland, defeated at North Brisbane, bas
received no encouragement to stand for his once-
safe constituency of Cairns, where they take off
their hats and address the Kanaka
as “Sir.” No Govt, supporter offers to retire in
Thomas .Joseph’s favor, and he has expressed a
vague desire to wrap his toga round himself and
retire from Assembly politics, but all the same he
has been nominated for Warwick (without his
knowledge, it seems), though Warwick is already
provided with a Ministerial candidate. To this
gentleman Byrnes has given a pretty plain hint
by saying that he couldn’t think of taking a seat
at the expense of another Ministerial supporter ;
but, so far, the gentleman is deaf. So that, at
time of writing, T.J.B. hovers round with his
toga half-on and half-off.
One thing which Sangster, M.L.A., seems
to have proved definitely is that the marine sur-
veyors of the Vic. Underwriters’ Association hold
their offices solely at the discretion of the ship-
owners whose vessels they have to report upon.
Which raises the question: how long will any
average crowd of shipowners keep a surveyor who
tells them with brutal frankness when their ships
are rotten and unfit to put to sea ? Of course no
shipowners would sack a man for telling the
plain, unvarnished truth, but one surveyor, who
alleges that he condemned the Alert and the Julia
Percy as unsafe vessels, was “l’emoved” shortly
after for want of tact and various other things
possibly quite unconnected with any beastly otit-
spokenness on his part. Meanwhile, some other
surveyor apparently passed these vessels. The
Alert went forth and foundered, while the Julia
Percy got through one gale by the millionth part
of a hair—measuring the hair round the thick part,
or stomach.

THEFT OF A WINDSOR UNIFORM.
The gilt and glamor—especially the gilt—
that hedges round a Court costume, caused
the annexation on Friday by soma person
unknown of Sir Henry Parkc-s's Windsor
uniform. It is supposed that the parcel
containing the uniform was stolen from a
buggy in Pitt-street. A gentleman was
taking the parcel to a tailor to be attended
to, and it is supposed to have been ab-
stracted from the buggy when he pulled
up for a few minutes to speak to a Wend
tn Pltt-street. The police on Saturday
found the coat at a second-hand dealer's,
who stated that ha had paid a very mode-
rate price for It. The remainder of the
uniform has not as yet been found.

It is the pnde of the miller and baker to
produce white flour and white bread ; it is the pride
of the J rame Food Co. to restore to the public in
a cheap and palatable form those important con-
stituents of the grain which, in the production of
white flour, are simply wasted and thrown away.
Ihe mnermost kernel of the grain contains the
white starchy matter, while the husk is rich in
albuminoids and phosphates, which may be less
sightly but are of the greatest nutritious value.
I hey are however, firmly embedded in the woody
fibre of the husk, so much so that the human
system is powerless to extract them or obtain any
benefit from them when introduced in the shape of
whole-meal bread, which may even do positive
arm to a weak stomach. The Frame Food Com-
FnJ'iho Y° n - •’ \ vbo , have now a representative
01?le V n i Mr - J \- Fich - of 41 York-street,
Sydney claim to have solved the great problem of
saving these, the most valuable parts of the cereals,
Fh? «?ii Pr< ; Sentm u g r th T in such a form tlia * even
!ral;!! k f S Om u h of a baby win retain them, and,
judging from the great popularity their prepara-
» y f ln it seems likely that tfiey
will not be found wanting when tried.*
Fire Caused by Phosphorus.
Mr and Mrs. Stodart, of Ardonachie Station,
lately had an unpleasant experience. They
were returning home from Hamilton, when
Mrs. Stodart noticed smoke rising from under
the seat of the buggy and found that a parcel
of phosphorus, intended for poisoning rabbits,
was on fire. She at once seized the parcel and
flung it out of the buggy, and on' examination
ound that she had done so just in time to
prevent serious injury to herself and husband,
as the clothing of both was considerably burned.
Stationholders should use “ TOXA ” for
poisoning rabbits, as it will not ignite and is
safe amongst stock.*

the last survivor of the N.S.W. Bar of 1840.
On the late “ Salmon ” Wilson’s Victorian
estate <£41,634 probate-duty has just been paid.
Two members of the Australian Eleven
use hair-dye. Hair-dye deceives no one but the
man who uses it.

maker, appears to have dropped into an English
estate of <£4,750,000.

the first Anglican bishop to bike daily and gaily
in diocesan thoroughfares.

uniform years ago the old man might have had
better luck in his last days. Windsor uniform—
it is a disease.

lawyer-politician cannot return to Australia just
yet, having caught fever in India, or somewhere.

Fred. Piesse, M.L.A., the new W.A.
Railway Commissioner, began life as a telegraph-
operator. He operated to advantage in sandal-
wood and kangaroo-skins.
J. F. Hogan’s belated impressions of Aus
tralia (the book is two years behind its date) come
in for a severe slating in the Saturday Review.
Hogan gushes about the charms of Lady Parkes
the Second, as if she were still alive, ignoring the
existence of No. 3. Also, he is one election behind
in his history of N.S.W. politics.

A N.S. W. counsel once closed an address
to a jury with : “ Eor God’s sake, gentlemen, for
Christ’s sake, for my sake, acquit the prisoner ! ”
Since then he has been known as the third person
in the Trinity.
William Holden, of S.A. Register, who
is seriously ill, is the oldest working journalist in
Australia. He is over 80 years of age, and has
been a newspaper-man for o ver CO years.
Melb. County Court Judge Hamilton, to a
hesitating barrister who was pleading before him
last week : “Go on, man ! Don’t be shivering on
the brink, like the gentleman in the hymn-book.”
Amongst the aristocratic visitors to Cool-
gardie is Viscount Avonmore—whose father,
years ago, was defendant in Threlfall v. Yel-
verton, known in legal history as the “ Yelverton
case. ”
Three brothers, triplets, are working
together at Swift’s Creek, Vic. Their respective
Christian names are Livewell, Dowell and Diewell.
They are known locally as “ Li.” and “ Do.” and
“ Die.”
Had Speight curled-up after his last defeat
at law, David Syme intended to restore the reduc-
tion made in his employes’ salaries when the
famous action was in its first battle. A proper
kind of thanksgiving—had it come off.
Dr. Bland, who in 1818 was convicted of
libelling the Governor, fined £5O, and sentenced
to a year’s imprisonment, was the first person
whose remains were taken to the Necropolis from
the Sydney Redfern mortuary station.
Ex-Sheriff Ellis was the most interesting
identity at the Melb. opening of the Equitable
Life Ass. Society’s splendid offices. He occupied
a little shanty on the same site, corner of Collins
and Elizabeth streets, half-a-century ago.

course, Saul was ramping. Corrections appeared
next day.
