Clever Pariss Nesbit’s contention that he
is only an amiable critic of Premier Kingston is
burst-up by a recent speech of his, wherein he
talked of Kingston “ thinking he was bigger than
God Almighty, and sitting on us with that fat,
unwieldy carcase of his.” You never talk of “the
unwieldy carcase ” of your friend.
Perhaps that conception of quaint minds,
the “disappearance disease,” which is said to un-
create a man as suddenly as Adam was created,
has caught Dr. Herbert Henry Meyers, 36 years
old, sft. Din. high, dark hair and skin, dark
whiskers and moustache—as per portrait adjacent.
Dr. Meyers formerly practised at Carlton, Melb.,
later at Ulverstone, Tas„ and recently at Pilliga,
N.S.W. On 29th Feb. he wired to his wife in
Melb. that he was going to Sydney to buy some
furniture ; and on the 6th March he was seen in
Sydney. Since then he has vanished—some sus-
pect to America ; but his friends think this im-
possible. Anyone who should run across him is
asked to communicate at once with the N.S.W
police for the sake of his anxious wife and
Mrs. Trait, the more or less insane woman
arrested on a charge of murder at Ballarat the
other week, is supposed to have become madder
than usual through the City of Melbourne Bank
closure. A bundle of the bank’s notes was found
sewn ' n one °f her petticoats.

THE SMARTEST OF SYDNEY
AUCTIONEERS.
Zimmerman, on arrival at ’Frisco, ascribed
ais three defeats in Australia not to meeting
superior men, but to his weakened condition
owing to the trying weather ; also, to the bad
tracks. But it was matrimony which beat Zim-
merman in Australia. The parson, not Parsons.
Mr. G. P. Slade, once well-known in
Sydney as a solicitor, and of late years resident in
London, who died a few weeks ago, had long
suffered from blood-poisoning after influenza, and
had undergone several operations which so weak-
ened him that he succumbed. He had not been
able to leave Margate for nearly a year before he
died.
The member of the Vic. parliamentary
cricket-team who scored 46 out of the total of 82
put up the first innings against N.S. W., addressed
each of his fellow-players as “ Sir,” and, although
Vic. parliamentary free passes frank holders over
N.S.W. tram-lines, regularly bought and paid for
his tram-tickets—a thing no self-respecting M.P.
ever does.
Archbishop Carr, in his recently-published
book, “ The Church and the Bible,” quotes in
large type, against the Protestant controversialists,
the phrase, “ Throw plenty of mud and some of it
is sure to stick.” The Archbishop has possibly-
forgotten that the phrase was coined by Voltaire,
the heretic, and applied to the prelates of Dr.
Carr’s own church !
Twenty aboriginals in full war-paint were
schooled to receive Gov. Lamington on his arrival
in Queensland. The Governor should have been
landed on a secluded spot and received by nigs,
significantly grouped about a fire, over which was
suspended a pot just about large enough for a man
of His Excellency’s size.
Spagnoletti : “When you talk about the
most delicious estate in Victoria I consider it is
this one: The place which a doting husband
wreathed around his lovely wife, after which she
bolted with the groom. I suppose he had beauti-
ful legs, in the buckskin, while hubby’s were of
the Hopetoun order, which the tar described as a
purser’s shirt on a marlinspike. ”
Hon. Tom Loader was most touching in
the witness-box when lie referred to his wondrous
faith in the dead manager of the City of Melb.
Bank. “I must confess, though,” said the ex-
chairman, with a sigh of new-born sorrow, “ that
that confidence has been considerably shaken as
the result of this examination.” It must be ter-
rible to feel obliged to kick a corpse.

The most comical spectacle of the annual
Vie. military encampments is that of the rotund
and hairy Rev. Dr. Bevan, who is an honorary
chaplain. He wears the regulation little cap
perched on the top of his wild shock of white
hair, looking like a round match-box on a heap of
straw; andhis24-gallon-ca.sk iigure is enveloped
in a tight-fitting military frock-coat, liberally
l'rogged.
Somers Vine, having exhausted his Aus-
tralian stories and the variants thereof, is no
longer so frequent a visitor at Marlborough House
(writes a Bulletin correspondent from London) —
in fact, it is said that he has been cold-shouldered
lately by Tummy. The financial failure of the
Imperial Institute is partly attributed to his
mismanagement, and this is a dire offence.
But to do Vine justice, one must say that
he is not the only one who lias helped to muddle
the business ; his chief, little brain-slicking Abel,
is much more responsible than the brusque Somers,
who, with all his faults, has some originality as a
showman.
Gov. Hampden at Sydney blind-asylum
meeting:—
I had the advantage of a close acquaintance for many
years with the late Mr. Henry Fawcett. One Christmas-
time, at Cambridge, as we were walking out together in
the morning, he pointed out to me a tree that faced the
colleges, and said, “Is not that a fine cedar?” I could
not help turning round to him on the moment and say-
ing, “ Well, it is a very fine tree, but how is it that you
know it is so ? ” He said, “ Yer forget that I could see as
well as you can when 1 was an under; raduate at Cam-
bridge.” He lost his sight when he was 10 or 20 years of
age, his eyes having been shot out. I have taken the case
of Mr. Fawcett as an illustration to show you what is
possible to human endurance, and human ability, and
human courage.
Lord Richard Neville’s cabin library on
the Sunbeam “chiefly consists,” it is related in
the press, “of depressing works of the type of
‘ The Family Physician,’ ‘ Modern and Domestic
Medicine,’ ‘ Haydn’s Dictionary of Popular Medi-
cine,’ and other ponderous ton.es, calculated to
‘ give a name ’ and a gruesome significance to
every ache, real and imaginary.” After which
printed announcement, as it pouts to hypochon-
dria, Lord Richard will have a bad time at the
hands of the quacks. By every post he will be
asked “Young man, don’t you feel well?” and
whether he is suffering from “ symptoms akin to
the above.”
One of the most successful meetings Vic.
Freetrade apostle Max Hirsch (“made in Ger-
many ”) ever experienced was at Caledonia, a
Scotch mining township in Lilydale district. The
shire president, hight M‘Taggart, or words to
that effect, was in the chair, and introduced Max
as “Mr. M‘Hirsch,” whereat the concave upper-
lips of the assemblage softened palpably. But
when Max’s oratory got into full swing and he
reeled off his statistics and his well-known de-
nunciations of the Age and the Protective policy
and the landlord, the freckle-bespangled visages
warmed to the subject, and every one of Max’s
telling points was cheered to the echo. It was
the greatest conversion the Vic. Freetraders ever
effected. As the meeting broke up, a long, red-
haired, raw-boned Aberdonian remarked to his
mate, “ Aye, but it was a graund lecture, a graund
lecture.” “It was that,” responded. the other;
but whaur hae thees M‘Hirsch come frae? I
dinna ken a Seoatish ahkseent like hees at
a’, at a’.” “Aweel,” explained Rufus, “ Ah’m
theenking he’s frae Shutland.”
Another story of C. H. James’s prowess
with his hands. During the occasion of a visit t*
Queensland, James was sleeping at the homestead
of a prominent member of the Pastoralists’ As-oci-
tion, and in the middle of the night was awak< : d
by a sound as of someone breaking into his room.
James forgot for the instant that he was not in
his own 1 oorak mansion, and at once diagnosing
burglars, hopped out of bed, threw open the door,
and ‘ dealt it out ” to a roughly-clad person stand-
ing in the passage. The supposed burglar feH
heavily to take his lOsec., and James had just
dropped on top to secure the malefactor when he
remembered “ where he were,” and striking a
match revealed the recumbent form of a fat
capitalist weltering in his gore. The Melb. man
sneaked silently back to bed and said nothing,
his host spent all next day in bed getting over
the effects of a dastardly outrage by shearers.”
The truth leaked out soon, however, the squatter
taking his punching good-humoredly enough, but
telegraphing to every station on James’s route—
Look out for C. H. James ; he has burglar on
the brain.”

Advertisements in Sydney dailies for lost
black-and-tan dogs, &c., make bushmen smile.
Ihe other day a dog followed a mob of 7000
travelling sheep out of a Darling Diver town.
His owner walked after them the next day. At a
cross-road m the outskirts of the town he inquired
which road the sheep had taken. “Sheep?”
asked a sleepy-looking young woman, “ I don’t
remember any sheep passing by here yesterday.”
• 1’ 7/t’ m °th e r, ” interrupted a barefooted little
girl I saw sheep yesterday.” “Aye,” said the
mother, as I come to think of it, I believe the
sheep did pass here ; but I didn’t notice what
roa,d they took. ” The road crossed right in front
of her house, and 7000 sheep raise enough dust to
smother Sydney, Melbourne, and Adelaide !
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
2,6 of this issue.
Did Diana, the last of the Tooram (Vic.)
aborigines, had before her death joined the Salva-
tion Army and turned teetotaller. This to please
the popular Miss Mary Murray, who was then
canvassing hard for brother Jack in his parlia-
mentary campaign against the Conservative W.
Allan. At this time Jimmy Lee, a well-known
Irish J.P., “pegged out,” and Warrnambool
Society formed a select party to visit Lee’s house
in the evening and see for itself what a
wake was like. When Society had arrived and
drawn long faces, Miss Murray asked Diana to
show the ladies what she had learnt at the Army.
Diana at once held forth something like this
Lord say it prayer por Hitter Mully,
And pray por Mitter Lee ;
And say it prayer por Mary Mully,
And say it one por me.
Make it get him Mr. Mully
Belongin’ to plenty vote ;
Don’t let Allen beat it, Lord,
Him big one (blanky) goat.
Then Society left.
In our adveitising columns will be found
the announcement of three of the largest trade
exhibitions and markets in the world, which are
annually held at the Royal Agricultural Hall,
London, England, under the auspices and direct
patronage of all the leading firms in the respective
trades. It will be seen that the “Confectioners
and Bakers’ Exhibition ” takes place from Septem-
ber 18th to 25th ; the “ Grocery, Pro vision, Oil and
Italian Warehouse Traders’ Exhibition ” from
October 2nd to 9th ; and the “ Brewers’, Malt-
sters’, Mineral Water Manufacturers, ’ &0., Exhi-
bition and Market ” from Oct. 31st to Nov. Oth.
It will be readily understood that these gatherings
form the most favorable markets for all who desire
to purchase to the best advantage, and it would
be well for colonial and foreign firms to instruct
their various European agents to visit these Exhi-
bitions in due course and place their orders accord-
ingly. The Agricultural Hall is the largest build-
ing of its kind in England, and the volume of
commercial transactions effected during the pro-
gress of these three important organisations is
always phenomenal.

ASTONISHING, isn’t it, that eighty out of every hundred manufacturers of Hair Restorers are
positively bald ! Under the circumstances, it is not surprising that we are fast becoming a bald-
headed people ! “ Physician, heal thyself ! ” could be most appropriately applied to these impostors
and charlatans. I can destroy all parasital growth as it affects the hair directly, but THESE parasites
I cannot remove. Their life or death is in your hands. Hitherto they have reaped a rich harvest at
your expense. The result is the premature loss of your hair. You can stop their little game by
refusing to buy their nostrums. If you suffered from any ailment of body you would not consult a
bricklayer or a carpenter. You would go direct to a doctor who had made it a life’s study. Why
should you continue to use preparations that are manufactured by people who do not possess even an
elementary knowledge of the “ Hair and its treatment ?
Send two stamps for my Illustrated Pamphlet on the “Hair and its Treatment.” To all who
desire a good head of hair, it is worth its weight in gold. Every person who wishes to escape pre-
mature baldness cannot do better than use my
Marvellous Hair Restorer.

“ The Mercadool Land Cases.—Forty-third
Day.” The lawyers are gnawing the big bone
clean. And Blake, M.P., could finish the Midland
railway-arbitration in one-third the time !
How to rear a child is one of the greatest
problems that affect humanity ; full half the child-
ren die before reaching the early age of five years.
The obvious reason is, either that the children
have nothing to live for, or else that, although
wishing to live, there is not the proper sustenance
handy for them to live on. The fresh and lovely
child whose photo, appears in this issue has
clearly a great deal to live for, and muchly enjoys
her meals. She is the embodiment of innocence,
health, and happiness ; she lives on Arnott’s Milk
Arrowroot Biscuits —her mother, wise woman,
always saw that she got Arnott’s. Messrs. Arnott
have innumerable photos of lovely children
who, having all been reared on the famous bis-
cuits, combine plumpness with a heavenly ex-
pression. The firm, judged from this trustworthy
evidence, has solved the whole problem of rearing
beauteous infants in this country. And, since the
race that fills the cradle rules the world, mothers
are advised to keep Arnott’s biscuits, so that the
cradle will always remain full—that is, won’t be
prematurely empty. One fact which mothers
should remember is that all the milk used in the
manufacture of Arnott’s biscuits comes from the
firm’s own dairy, which is supervised by experts—
a circumstance which deserves the highest com-
mendation. As time goes on the photos of many
children, certified to be reared on Arnott’s biscuits,
will appear in this paper; and their names and
addresses will also appear as a guarantee of good
faith.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*

Brisbane Courier brags that Q. elections
are “ a triumph over Socialism.” But in several
cases the Labor-candidates polled few less than the
Ministerialists. At Toovvong, for example, lorn
Finney started with 200 property-votes to Ins dis-
credit, and yet Reid was only beaten by four.

PRESS NOTICES OF “IN THE DAYS WHEN THE
WORLD WAS WIDE.”
“ How graphic he is, hiw natural, how true, how strong!
. . . Lawson’s keen sympathy, his knack of observa-
tion, are characteristically feminine. His sense of humor,
his talent for vivid portrayal, are as characteristically
masculine. Yet it is the woman in him, as in others,
that makes his talent glow to the white heat of genius.”—
The Bulletin.
“ The verses have natural vigor, the writer has a rough,
true faculty of characterisation, and the book is racy of
the soil from cover to cover. . . Space forbids anything
in the way of quotation, and we can do no more than call
attention to the breezy sweep of such lines as those in
‘ln the Days when the World was Wide,’ the pitiless
realism of ‘ Out Back,’ the quaint moralising in ‘ For’ard,’
the grim fatalism of ‘Taking his Chance,’ the reflective
insight in ‘ Faces in the Street,’ the ring of ‘ The Roaring
Days,’and the hopeless note of monotony in ‘The Great
Grey Plain.’ ” — Sydney Morning Herald.
“ This is emphatically a book to buy, to read, and to
re-read with ever-recurring pleasure.”— New Zealand
Mail.
“It is difficult to select stanzas from a set in which all
are superb.”— The Champion.
“ There is a touch of deeper feeling and significance in
Mr. Lawson’s compositions which should ensure them
cordial acceptance by thoughtful readers.”—The Cosmos
Magazine.
“ There is much in this book that the world will be the
better and the richer for. Theie are grand songs in it,
with a cheery swing, that are full of the odor of the gum-
bush, that are resonant with the sounds of Australian life.
There are stories full of love, pathos and beaut}', such as
give a man a lump in his throat. An Australian book it
is, full of the spirit of our land, a book written, printed
and published in Australia, and every man ought to have
it in his library.”— Sydney Stock and Station Journal.

Gaul us, winner of Hawkesbury Handicap and
Rosehill Cup, was always regarded as a hot pickle. Under
the circs., his stable was lucky in getting long odds both
times. His dam, Industry (a Musket), was a good jigirer
in her day.
* * * *
Four Australian jockeys practically killed within
a week.
* * * *
W. R. Wilson is one of the few owners who like
chestnuts. Wallace is the best he ever bred. The St.
Alban's color-prejudice extends only to greys.
* * * *
Wallace eased to fives for Sydney Cup in the
week, consequent on a run on Toreador, who wont from
tens to sevens. Patroness and Acmeiia were the best
backed of the weaker sex. Weight of money has made
Courallie the Doncaster favorite at time of writing, touch-
ing eights. Stable commissions favored Jack o’ Lantern,
True Blue and Inspiration same dav.
» * * •
The Hova crowd is very reticent as to its
horso’s in ivements. Should he come to Sydney the Mile
All-Aged Stakes is a moral if he can, as alleged, break
1.40. If Adelaide is decided on, he ought to'sweep the
w.f.a. hoard there.
* * * *
Norton failed to place himself in Liverpool
Grand National, but, according to an advice last mail,
may shape better in the Prince of Wales steeple, run at
Sandown Park this week.
* « * »
Lizette’s Caulfield record jump, 37ft., has been
equalled by Hopeful (half-brother to Mon Ami) at Hamil-
ton (Vic.). Chandler (Eng.) made such another leap once
over a water-jump.
* * * *
The highest Australian jump made by a lady,
in saddle, is sft. 9in., put up by Mrs. Stace on the Brisbane
horse Anyhow, at Tenterfield show on March 11.
* * * *
Certain it is that the bigger the owner the more
necessity for sweep-horse holders to lay a “ hit to no-
thing.” Perhaps it is that they can better afford to
“ bite their nose off to spite their face.”
* * * *
Strange that Mr. Figg’s two trotters, Tuxedo
and Lady Era, should each have the playful habit of
throwing the driver out of the sulky. Tuxedo came with
that reputation from England. Could the Lady have
learned the trick from him ?
* * * *
The steeplechasers entered for the A.J.C. meet-
ing are a very ragged lot, and there doesn’t seem much
prospect of N.S.W. neddies shining in the winter inter-
provincial contests. The only new name is Fishmonger,
a sturdy M.L. lepper, who has a better record than any,
bar Waterbury.
* * * *
Outside each of the “ closed ” Sydney tote-shops
you may still see a solitary figure with a bunch of tickets
m one hand and a pencil in the other. If you happen to
be a tall, hairy person with large flat feet, your application
for a half-crown ticket.will be met with a vacant stare;
but if you are an undersized, clean-shaved person with a
soft feit hat and bow legs, you may back your fancy to
your heart’s content.
* * ♦ *
People have a notion that it is impossible to live
by backing horses. How is it then that at every Sydney
race-meeting—and, counting the night meetings at' Lillie
Bridge, there are eight every week —you may see the
same faces from one year’s end to the other? They are
not bookmakers; they are mere punters. These people
must live, and do live, and, judging from appearances,
live well. Now, how do they do it ?
* » * *
A certain Q. bookie got a surprise the other day.
When double-laying he bawled into a passing owner’s ear,
“ This and the next!” getting a bang on the nose in
reply. “ What’s that for ?” he enquired furiously. “That’s
for ‘this,’ and this” (hitting again) “is for the next
blanky time you try to stiffen my jockey with a fiver.”
* * * *
The tote often can’t register the crowd that
compete in hack events on the West Coast of northern
M.L., so horses are bracketed together. If one so
bracketed wins, and the other runs last, the latter pays a
div. just the same. Backing a losing winner is new in its
way.
* * * *
Apropos of forestalling. When big books were
the vogue in M.L. two smarties with some ready money
rushed a certain horse for a handicap, got a few thousands
and made him favorite. The owner, being well-nigh
“broke,” had to start the neddy, which won. Not a
oopper was laid him by the clever pair. That owner,
however, now relates, apparently with grim satisfaction,
how one of the two died in M. L. through drink, while his
mate perished on a parched and thirsty Australian plain
for the want of it.
* * * *
How is it that Ikey Mo has not yet obtained a
footing on bowling-greens to wager on the singles, pairs,
handicaps, whisky pools and all the rest of it ?
» * * *
M.L. gambling-law. A welshed punter com-
plained of the disappearance of a mushroom-bookie, who
had laid him tote-odds. X 4110 told the victim to hold
his tongue : he had made himself liable to a £2O fine for
accepting tote-odds from anyone but the machine itself.
* * * *
Someone finds that there is a law of George 111.
which prohibits pony-racing, and this has not been
repealed in N.S. Wales. It may be used against the Ken-
sington and other clubs, but public opinion will not
support such tactics.
* * * *
A crowd of Adelaide “books” were recently
heavily fined for wagering in the streets, without even
.jailing the odds. As the courts now construe the Totali-
sator Act, any betting except through the machine is
absolutely illegal. In fact, if Moses Cohenstein merely
whispers through his nose to Ikey Goldsmidt that “ it’s
two to one it’ll rain to-morrow ” the police instantly hang
on to his collar and have him fined £5. Very soon an
Adelaide “ bookie ” will only be valuable as a specimen
for the museum.
* * * *
Griffo, the miniature Tom Sayers, must now be
very nearly at the end of his tether. He was not long
since run in for drunkenness in Yankeeland, and his
friends had to raise the 2dol. fine. Dissipation and im-
pending legal proceedings compelled him to refuse an
offer of £BOO to appear at the big Mexico boxing carnival,
from all which comfort may be derived with the thought
that each outbreak makes a return to his native city less
likely.
* * * *
The way Jim Hall played with Choynski while
his condition lasted shows what a pugilistic career the
long Australian threw away by a life of riotous living.
Hall was the coolest local boxer the writer ever saw-
reminding one much of Jem Mace in his palmy days,
never doing an ounce of superfluous work with head,
hand; or foot, always just out of distance for his adver-
sary’s blow, but timing his own to a nicety. And Hall
resembles Mace also in the possession of a remarkably
weak spot. As a stripling, the writer saw Hall lie down
before a man he was able to thrash out of hand, and from
that day could never stand the long fellow as a world-
beater.
* * ♦ *
There are some big Melb. football meetings in
May. Melbourne v. Essendon, on the 2nd, opens the
season ; Fitzroy (last year’s premiers) v. Essendon, on the
16th ; Fitzroy v. Melbourne, on the 25th (for Queen’s birth-
day). June 6th is set apart as a day for oharity matches
—a new departure.
* * * *
Though talked of last year, Sydney Rugby
Union has made no sign re adopting electorate football.
This hugger-mugger policy Is losing the Union much-
needed dollars. Were electorate the vogue, public inter-
est in strongly-contested amalgamated locality matches
would be trebled ; receipts accordingly. The scheme is
perfectly workable, and players would fall into line at
once. But the Union appears chary about leaving the old-
fashioned track. Under the new idea, too, clubs, enjoying
a much stronger membership, would be "able to acquire
professional teachers and proper appliances requisite for
an all-round athletic training, and scientific field exhibi-
tions would result. Also, young members would enjoy
all advantages, and secure promotion if merited. At
present, unless a rising player has committee-inliuence,
the first 15 is beyond his ken.
The amalgamation of the Balmain f.c’s will
enable that locality to be very strongly represented this
season. It is officially stated, too, that the Wentworth's
have “arranged ” to import three dandies from Maoriland.
Which is unique in the way of commercial enterprise,
though rough on local talent. There will be a few notice-
able eliminations from the Pirates, but plenty of good
material is obtainable North Shorewards. Other clubs are
also procuring the best kicking tackle available.
Sydney f. clubs aren’t noted for their “cash pro-
fessionalism.” Three last-season players used to receive
2s. each for a Saturday-night razzle —if their side won,
which wasn’t oiten. Otherwise, they merely got mono-
tonous street-corner sympathy.
* * * *
For the first time since its formation, Melb.
Mercantile R.C. succeeded in winning a senior eight-oar
contest, when on Saturday last the Vic. championship fell
to its crew. The race was a very open one, the Mercs.,
Yarra, Albert Park, and Tamar (Tas.) eig'hts being, as far
as practice was concerned, practically equal; and so little
money changed hands. The winners were stroked by A.
B. Sloan, the brilliant Fitzroy footballer ; and R. E.
Dawson, the “ cash amateur ” cyclist, rowed No. 3. Four
of the remaining six were junior and maiden oarsmen,
but they all pulled like a crew of years’ experience, and
won by a length and a-half, alter a two-mile tussle with
the Yarra, in 17min. 27sec. But the so-called champion-
ship is an empty honor, for the Wendouree R.O. (Ballarat)
could donkey-lick the winners.
* * * *
Tom Kelly, trainer of Beach and others, docs
not understand the statement that he was to go to
England with Stanbury. He never had any such
intention, and is strongly of opinion that Jim will
be beaten, owing to his tendency to make flesh
even here. Pearce and Kemp both lost in England to
men they “could carry and win from” here, by reason of
adiposity, and Kelly thinks Stanbury the hardest man to
train we have had. Jim’s day’s “ work ” has always been
phenomenal in its severity, and yet he had great trouble to
keep the flesh in abeyance. Veteran Kelly says that the
best man he ever did see was Donald M’Donald, of
Grafton, who, allowing for the fact that he only scaled
lOst. 41b., was, Kelly thinks, the fastest and certainly
the prettiest sculler ever known anywhere.
• * * *
The trade gag “ that anybody without a bicycle
is nobody ” doesn’t quite fit the case ; “ anybody on a
bicycle is nobody” is the correct expression. Anyone
who doesn’t think so should watch the butcher-boy pass-
ing Gov. Hampden in the Centennial Park.
* * * *
The whirligig of (the) time—the bicycle.
* * * *
A Melb. biker the other day, in a three-and-a-
half-mile run on St. Kilda road, passed the Guvmenthouse
crowd (five), three doctors, four lawyers, four M. L.A’s,
six society ladies, a butcher in his blues, a lamplighter
with his pole, a carpenter with some joinery work on his
handles, and two Chinese —one of them a laundryman
taking home “welly ni’ iloned shirtee.” And the Chows
rode as well as any in the crowd.

man, and having afterwards forsaken this machine, has
now been downed by Martin on a Humber.

One of the most dismal courts ever held in
N.S.W. has been sitting at Broken Hill—the
Land Board Court, making new appraisements of
rabbit-smitten, drought-stricken, mortgage-laden
runs that, in any case, would have had a tough
struggle against the collapse in the market-values
of wool, sheep, &c. The witnesses have been
chiefly station-managers and representatives of
financial companies who have sought to show that
the rabbits converge on their own particular
station and stay there ; while, moreover, generally
speaking, wild dogs are worse on each man’s own
holding than anywhere else, and both rabbits and
dogs are particularly difficult to cope with just in
his neighborhood. Also, in nearly every case, the
country is a peculiarly bad one to water, because
of the tendency to silt ; one or two, according
to the reports, have added that duststorms fill
up the tanks and cause the fences to disappear
under drift. Every man of them says that all the
grass and most of the edible scrubs and bushes are
gone ; and every man of them is also assured that
the scrubs and bushes will never grow again.
There is scarcely one that cannot show that his
losses during the last few years have been either
greater than or about equal to his lambings ; and
most of them have observed that the fleeces and the
frames of the sheep are diminishing—through the
eating out of the country by rabbits, they say.
And some of them give actual figures in support
of their contention. On Mount Arrowsmith, for
instance, the average weight of scoured fleece in
18<J0 was 31b. 50z., ’9l 31b. 20z., ’92 31b. loz., ’93
31b., ’94 21b. 15g0z., and ’95 21b. 13|lb. Generally
speaking, the depreciation in the carrying capacity
of the West Darling country since the invasion of
rabbits —say in the last six or seven years— is de-
clared to be 50 per cent, or thereabouts (Crown
witnesses even admit that); while the fall in the
value of wool has been about 30 percent., and of
surplus sheep from 40 to 60 per cent. A hundred
and one other thingsjare also ascribed to the rabbit-
plague. Thus says one witness —“ The rabbits
have eaten out the grass roots, so that we get no
spring from an inch of rain like we used to do. It
takes over three inches now to do much good,
because the spring has to come from seed. ” And
another: “ The fleeces are getting dirtier and
dirtier. I attribute this to the eating out of the
scrubs. The dirtier the fleece the greater the loss
in carriage. The eating out of the scrubs and
grass-roots also increases the tendency of tanks to
silt.” Rabbit-net the country, of course, answers
the man who knows all about Eastern and Cen-
tral squattages. But men who have tried it on
this sand-drifty country, where the rains, when
they do come, are patchy and of tropical severity,
aver that the cost of maintenance reaches £5 per
mile per annum ; and yet this country will not in
its best state carry more than perhaps one sheep
to eight acres. On Gnalta, which was three years
ago wire-netted at a cost of about £5OOO, the
rabbits are almost as bad as they are outside, des-
pite continuous expense in poisoning and main-
tenance. Remembering that Ear Western fleeces
are light, the wool only of moderate character, and
the cost of carriage high, and that hundreds of
thousands of acres of country are unoccupied, this
“ net the paddocks ” solution of the problem will
be seen to have weak features about it.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-'street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery ; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
N.S.W. Railway * Commissioner Fehon’s
Corowa sheep-station, in the West Darling district,
is probably the best wild-dog run in Australasia.
The losses of sheep from the attacks of dogs are
estimated at 5 per cent, of the total flock per
annum. During the last five years 1452 of the
vermin have been killed and accounted for.
Messrs. D. Braham and Co. have removed
their cellular clothing depdt to much larger and
more convenient premises at 384 George-street,
Sydney, between King-street and the G.P.O.
The boom in cellular clothing had quite outgrown
the narrow limits of the firm’s old establishment,
but it is hoped that the new premises, which are
of great dimensions, and situate in the very centre
of the business part of the city, will accommodate
it for a long time to come.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers

Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Hear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W, O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Eriday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

Johannesburg has got an unemp oyecl pro-
blem. Hundreds of men are seeking work of any
sort at Kafir wages.— Cape Argus.
Received, by a Melb. registry-keeper :
That cook you sent me last month was a fraud. She
came back from the township at one o’clock this morning
terribly tipsy and sinking like a maniac. When I asked
her to get up at 8 o’clock she said she would see me d d
nrst, and at dinner to-day, when I scolded her for burning
the custard, she threw it in the fire, and called me a
nagging old , and said all my brains were in my feet.
i wish you would be more careful in making your
selections.”
“I require an active man for hotel work. Duties —rise
at o a.m., clean boots, light fire for cook, and milk four
cows. After breakfast scrub out bar, passages, clean
windows and stables. After dinner chop wood and make
himself useful for the rest of the day. No work after
8 p.m. Wages, Bs. a week.”
“ I would like you to get me a respectable, educated
man used to farm work. He will have to milk 20 cows,
use scythe, split rails, and plough a little. I want him
also to teach my two boys (age 9 and 13) reading, writing,
and arithmetic at night; and as I am a widower I will
expect him to give a hand with the cooking. The wages
are 12s. a week. I would prefer a man that knows some-
thing about stump-grubbing.”
“ Will you kindly engage for me a married couple. Man
must be thoroughly competent to take charge of small
dairy farm ; wife to make butter, and look after pigs and
poultry. They will have to find their own bread, tea, &c.,
but can have milk, butter, and vegetables free. I expect
to get a reliable couple under these conditions for 17s. 6d.
a week.”
‘‘Will you please send me a respectable young man for
light work. He will have a comfortable home, and 12s. (id.
a wee k- He will be asked to wash the children and drive
them to school, and to assist in the kitchen, also to look
after two ponies, and keep things tidy.”
“ Will you please look me out a tutor for my three boys,
aged 10, 13, and 15 respectively. He must be a Protestant,
and will have to teach reading, writing, and book-keeping,
etc. He will live with us and not in the men's hut, but I
expect him to find his own blankets. Also, I expect him
to be civil and obliging, and to help with everything he
may be asked to. I can forgive most things but laziness,
that I can’t forgive. The last tutor I had was a new-chum
Englishman, a good teacher but the dirtiest blackguard
ever I met, and was so cunning that it took me a long
time to find him out, but when I did I kicked him off in
double-quick. So be sure and send me a respectable man.
Salary, £26 per year.”
TO DARKEN GREY HAIR.
Lockyer’s Sulphur Hair Restorer, quickest,

bottles Is. fid. everywhere.*
Dear Bulletin, — The saw-mill industry
would form a training-school for Australia’s de-
fence-forces unequalled as a test of courage and
stoical endurance of wounds and abrasions. Gone
broke following a stringer on the diggings, I was
offered work at a local mill, to start next morning.
Surely the eye of man never saw a more battered
crew than those, my future mates. The hench-
man owned with honest pride that he had twice
been reported killed by timber flying off the saw —
once Maoriland way, and again in Echuca (Vic.)
His fingers, what there was of them, were hacked
about, and stuck out from the palm in every direc-
tion save that in which they had originally grown.
In recounting his various boxing-matches with the
circular, he always pointed out how lucky he was
not to have had more of himself sawn up. The
“ tailer-out ” didn’t have so many honorable scars
about his head, but the ends were off most of his
.fingers, and three were totally gone, ground to pieces
under the rack-bench. The hoppy gait of this
veteran was explained by the fact that all the toes
were gone from his right foot, also the big one on
the left. He explained it “ was lucky ” that his
foot was “ sort of turned,” or he would have had
no toes at all when the “ flitch ” fell off the “ skid.”
In the morning four of us mustered up ; one, a
new-chum, whom I had not previously seen, had
his head tied up in a blood-wet rag. He had been
getting out of the way of some other form of
sudden death, and had fallen into the sawdust
hole at the little bench. The saw had chawed a
bit out of his ear. The other two fellows thought
it “ was lucky ” that his head had not come fair
on to the saw, but the chum, being new to the
track, did not seem sufficiently thankful. He had
a “ Just before the battle, mother,” air about him
that was depressing. Gladiators saluting Caesar
is the only parallel to our ‘ ‘ good morning ! ” as
we filed past the boss. Saws had been running
about five minutes when a piece of timber 10ft.
long, 4in. x 2in., went like a spear over my head
and drove a hole through the iron roof. The new-
chum had allowed the scantling to touch the back
of the saw. As I put on my coat and left, I heard
the boss say “Some men don’t want work.”—
R.A.F. Q.

It, was at “old Paul’s” on the Murrum-
bidgee. Two bush youths, seated on a form in
the bar, were singing vociferously “Rule
Britannia,” when an old lag, half-seas over,
emerged from an inner recess. “What! Britons
never shall be slaves ! You dogs ! I’ve been
ploughing in Tasmania, yoked up like a
bullock, and you tell me Britons never shall be
slaves ! I’ll knock your heads together,
you —! ”
The New Guinea cigarette-holder, pipe or
“ bow-bow ” consists of a hollow bamboo from two
to three feet long, closed at one end and open at
the other. Near the closed end a small hole is
made in the bamboo, and in this a lighted cigarette
is inserted. From the open end the native takes
a long draught of smoke ; but, instead of blowing
it out, blows it back into the pipe ; hands the
calumet to his comrade, who then takes a whiff of
the already-used smoke through the hole in which
the cigarette was inserted. This operation is
repeated by the next pair of natives, and is con-
tinued in turn through the social circle—women
and children participating in the luxury. It is
only civilised man who is so unsociable as to want
a pipe all to himself.
Offenders sentenced to imprisonment under
14 days in Gidyatown do their time in the local
lock-up. The old lock-up keeper, Sergt. Mullarty,
is single and keeps no cook, and the prisoners get
three square meals a day from the opposite pub.
“Three Star Frank” came into the town with
a cheque, and wasn’t there long before he stood
before the magistrate charged with being drunk
and disorderly. “ ,£1 or 14 days.” Frank pre-
ferred to “ take it out.” He knew what sort of a
boarding-house he was going to. “ Your Wurr-
shop,” protested the sergeant, “he’s got the
money. Oi’ve got noine pounds of his at the
station.” “ Can’t help it,” said the Bench, “we
can’t make him pay; ” and the disappointed
sergeant was ordered to take the culprit away.
Arrived at the lock-up Mullarty gave up the £9,
opened the door and passed Frank out. “Get
out’er this, yer dirthy loafer ! It’s the loikes o’
you that gets the bleeding counthry into debt!
Go on away now ! ” That night Frank got
paralytically drunk again, and was “ performing”
when Sergeant Mullarty arrived upon the scene.
“If yer (hie) don’t leave me (hie) alone,”
stuttered the lover of “three stars,” “I’ll (hie)
come back for them (hie) 14 days yer owe me.”
Whereupon Sergeant Mullarty “gat.”
Essay on Yictoria by a nine-year-old Melb.
boy : —■
Victoria is the lowest colony of Australia. Some people
say the climate is salubrous, but these people are wrong
altogether. The sudden changes from heat to cold, and
vesa-versa, are awfull, the thermometer often dropping 50
degrease in a couple of hours. The soil is very fertile,
producing grain, fruit, gold, sheep and cattle in great
abundans. Victoria has many strange animals, including
the wonderfull kangeroo and platipuss. The platipus,
which lives mostly on eels, has a beutiful skin, softer than
a cat’s, a bill like a duck, and paws like a human-being.
The kangaro walks uprite, and can balance itself on the
tip of its tail, which is often 10 feet long. When chased,
it has been known to jump a river 20 yards wide. The
flying-fox is exactly like a bat, only 50 times larger, it lives
on gum leaves and bees, and has deadly poisonus claws.
Another queer animal is the native bear, which sleeps in
the day time hanging by its tale from a branch. It lives
mostly on lisards and is very prevalent in some parts.
Melbourne the capital is situate at the mouth of the Yarra
which smells awful, it has run up very quick and forty
years ago had only about 20 houses most of which were
tents. Now it has buildings 20 storys high. Much money
though has been wasted in useless buildings such as the
Exibition, Parliament and Govement houses. There
are many fine places of amusment, including theatres,
the Zoo, churches, waxwurks, hotels, the morgue, and
cathedrils. Most Melbourne people have little money
nowadays, having been swindeld out of it by the banks,
etsetera.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inforin our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TZHUELZEIE CASTLES” and tc CAPS X’AAESL. 75
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to .the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. {Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

In an English paper we find a professor of
mathematics describing a theory thus :
If you bored a hole through the earth—say, roughly,
from New York to Melbourne —and dropped your gold
watch into that hole, it would go right through to Mel-
bourne, and immediately start on its return journey to you.
Would it? Is it possible in this so-called 19th
century, five years after the boom, that a learned
British professor knows so little of Melbourne? /

A returning miner, writingto aN.S. Wales M.P.
describes Coolgardie as the goldfield of many disappoint-
ments ; sickness is the only thing that is plentiful; 90 per
cent, of the mines are duffers, and the balance will pay
only after big money for plant and water. He thinks the
Eastern Govts should do their best to stop the mad rush
The Bulletin thinks so, too. It rises to propose
that the Eastralian Premiers should send a joint cable to
Chamberlain warning the British public that more than
nine-tenths of the W.A. mines floated in London are
proved \v lid cats, and that there is every reason to
believe that evgn this proportion will be exceeded if the
boom is not stopped. What business of theirs? Well
Australasia will have to suffer for these gigantic robberies!
The fact that the bulk of the plunder remains in London
does not affect the issue.

to keep away save the trained miner, and even he is ur fr ed
to come in moderation. “ Even if a man has means there
is no business to engage in. The Chosen People are there
as plentiful as swallows in summer, and what they are
disposed to leave alone in the way of genteel easy occupa-
tions the Unchosen could not earn water au”

* * * * *
Broken Hill Water Supply Co. has received a
knock from which it will take many months to recover.
The Broken Hill municipality selected a sylvan retreat
among the hills for its nightsoil depot, said retreat being
between the town and the creek. Opinions have always
been divided as to whether the site did not actually form
P ar ri “*? watershed ; and, while holding that it didn’t,
the Council salved its conscience by building an embank-
ment at the bottom of the gully. But, a week or two
ago, heavy thunderstorms came, burst the embankment,
and carried the material for the trenches certainly in the
direction of the creek. The interested authorities pro-
claim that “ none of it reached the reservoir ” (which, by
the way, has more water in it than ever it had before,
these particular storms having added over 400 million
gallons), but that “it was deposited over the plains.”
That may be true ; but, whether it is or not, while people
have a drop of another brand of water they certainly
won t be taking any from the co.’s service.
* * * *
The stacks of ore on the British grow steadily,
shipments having ceased as the finishing-touches are put
upon the big concentrating mill. But, though late
developments have been satisfactory, the ore is, com-
paratively speaking, patchy, both as to quantity and
quality.

* * » »
Barrier miners are apparently recovering from
the defeat of ’92. They have had a little strike at the
Pinnacles dragging wearily on, over just some such
trumpery matter as has more than once hitherto brought
trouble to this unfortunate property. A good deaf of
pegging-out has, by the way, been done in the Pinnacles
district lately ; Pritchard Morgan has been introduced to
that part.
* * * *
Horn Island lies close to Thursday, and not a
rifle-shot from the well-advertised Hammond Island bat-
tery. Nine-tenths of the miners on Horn Island are backed
by Thursday Island business men, officials, &c. A five-head
stamper battery erected by Mr. A. Rodgers has done good
work there lately. The first noteworthy crushing came
from the Eclipse—ll2oz. from 30 tons ; the Triumph
crushed 100 tons for an average of l£oz. ; the Bonanza
turned out 15 tons for 450 z., while the Good Luck crushed
60 tons for 60oz. Working miners, unless they can lie
back a couple of months, need not go there, as prices are
stiff (corned beef sd. a lb., steak 6d.) and work for the
next month or two will be partially suspended.
* » * »
The colored-labor question is causing trouble at
Horn Island. A welt-known Indian applied to the
warden for a miner’s right, which was refused for the
time being. Ultimately the authorities decided it should
be granted, inasmuch as the applicant was a British sub-
wet. Affairs now stand thusly—any South Sea Islander
born under British rule may claim the “ right,” and, as
some of the claims on Horn Island are already manned
by Polynesians, there is trouble brewing for Northern
miners.
* * * *
Re lucky diggers : When Gymoie broke out the
two Lords, Bob and Fred, bought a claim for £2 from a
party who had sunk 14ft. on the Caledonian line and got
nothing. The Lords set to work and sank a new shaft,
and one day, during a storm, the one who was on top
whilst his brother was working below, strolled up to the
old shaft, and on a heap of wet mullock lying at its
mouth he found some gold. He picked up a few oz., and
next day they began work at the old shaft and found that
the former owners had sunk right on to the gold before
abandoning it, and, in fact, had stripped a couple of feet
of the reef the whole length of the shaft. This reef had
been washed by the water when it ran down the shaft,
and gold was showing all over it. In three weeks the
u-u ~ o£ a bout 2000 oz. of specimens, and one of these,
which was sent to Sydney to be exhibited, contained over
" v! Sold. In a month this run of gold was worked out,
and besides the specimens they got 120 tons of crushing
stone which yielded lOoz. to the ton, making in all about
3200 oz.—won in about six weeks on an original investment
of £2.
* » * *
Summary of Queensland’s gold productions:—
Charters Towers goldfield (area, 320 square miles) has,
since discovery, to 1895 yielded 3,363,0000 z., of the
value of £12,000,000 sterling. Gympie comes next
with 2,000,0000 z. ; then Rockhampton, with 1,470,0000 z. ;
Palmer, 1,350,0000 z. ; Etheridge (area, 400 square miles),
530,0000 z. ; Calliope, Hodgkinson, &c., 780,0000 z. Alto-
gether the record for Queensland up to 1895 equals
10,625,0000 z. of gold—value, £39,843,750. “ Westralia,”
said a Northern miner indignantly, the other day, “why,
we’ve got a Westralia in our tailings ! ”
* * # *
Gold has hitherto been the chief Victorian
mining 1 industry, and accordingly its mining acts have
been framed with especial reference to that branch. Now
that coal-mines are studding Gippsland, a more general
mining law is badly needed. The Regulation of Mines
Act happens to include coal-mines, but the phraseology of
the sections isn’t always apropos ; the Mines Act proper
only applies to mining on Crown lands, whereas nearly all
the coal is on private lands. A colliery explosion is not
the most desirable means of drawing attention to serious
omissions in a country’s legislation. Agitation is all the
more necessary, because the subject does not immediately
interest the purely mining M’sP., and only incidentally
the agricultural Gippsland members.
* * * *
The Chinese are rapidly eating up Gippsland.
Wherever there is a bit of alluvial in a creek there is a
settlement of Chows fossicking assiduously for the last
weight., and getting it. This is rough on white pro-
spectors, who have hitherto been able to fall back upon
the creeks for tucker when hard pressed in their hunt for
the reef. The obnoxious Chow does not prospect, he never
pioneers, and his depredations tremendously handicap the
whites who do.
* * * *
With about half its batteries idle, Bendigo is
awaking to the idiocy of equipping each mine with
crushing machinery. Where haulage is easy, and at
Bendigo it generally is, a big central mill would do the
woik muchjnore cheaply and effectively,
Tasmania’s past five years’ mineral output :
Gold, 382,1380 z., £1,435,724; silver ore, 53,954 tons,
£557,030 ; tin, 15,198 tons, £1,207,776 ; coal, 177,154 tons,
£152,917 —’95 the lowest, £612,401; ’94 the best, £820,267.
February s yields were close on £50,000, an increase of
£BOOO on the previous month.
* * * *
Great interest is being displayed in the Glencoe
experiment at Croydon (Q.), which will settle the value of
the treatment there of big low-grade ore bodies by dry
crushing and cyanidation.
* * * *
Mt. Lyell railway due for opening in July next.
* ♦ * *
The agitation for State batteries grows in Vic-
toria with the spread of prospecting. It has been a Govt,
policy for years, since the first bitterness of boom after-
math, to rush men on to settlement, or set them gouging
mother-earth after quartz reefs. Now, those triers who
have been fortunate enough to hit stone are either on
their beam-ends for the want of a mill, or are compelled
to see all their profits scooped by carters and mill-owners.
Mines-Minister Foster declares he is anxious to dot the
country with little mills, but cannot see how it is
to be done on a business basis. Prospecting in good
districts would receive a tremendous impetus by the ap-
pearance of public mills, where crushing would be well
done at reasonable rates. Without such assistance, scores
of small reefs, in Gippsland particularly, must remain un-
workable for years, or until the gold-saving appliance
comes along.
A Jingo Hymn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Consider this Empire we have built up : what manner
of men the builders be, &c. &c. -Brisbane Courier
Llnee burly men of British breed and British
blood are we
(Now, one’s a Paddy, one’s a Scot, and one’s a
Johnny B.) ;
From yonder famous isle we came, set deep in
\\ estern Sea
(Our boots are made in Paris, and our hats in
Germanee).
In rough many a rover runs our blood from
ancient Vikings down
( Lhe Pole, the Jew, the German fill the half of
London town) ;
Our navy rules the rolling main from Zetnbla
to Peru
(And Norsemen, Danes, and Dutchmen form the
sturdy British crew) ;
Our Empire runs its iron ring round many a
distant strand
(Our eggs and butter mostly come from Den-
mark’s little land) ;
Our flag, that floats so proudly, braves the
battle and the breeze
(Our grain is grown in Yankeeland, and Holland
sends us cheese).
lull many a hero tall we’ve raised, and many a
tuneful bard
(We’ve left off raising pigs and beeves and
mutton-chops and lard) ;
Our proud possessions dot the deep of many a
tropic main
(Our fruits are all from Florida and Portugal
and Spain).
Small wonder that we worship it—the land
where Shakespeare sung
(One half our folk don’t know his name—thev
worship Beer and Bung) ;
The land it is where Freedom dawned—a land
both rich and rare
(Where peer and pedlar join to crush the hap-
less proletaire).
And now, “ Long live the Queen,” sing all
whose hearts are leal and true
(lhe Queen ! But, stay ! the Gracious Guelphs
were “made in Deutschland,” too);
Well, then—long live the Income Tax, the Poor
Rates and the Debt !
Thank God, we made them all ourselves, and lo !
we have them yet !
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.

The Benefit of Clergy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As Father Connolly reached the door of the
Squatters’ Rest a dozen arms were stretched out
to hold his horse and help him alight.
“It’s a hot ride you’ve ’ad, sure, father?” said
the buxom proprietress.
“ Faith, hot’s no name for it, Mrs. Dargan. Is
there anything left in my bottle ? ”
“ Well, it’s close on four months since you was
’ere, yer reverence; but I’ve got some good old
Irish tack ye’re welcome to.”
“That’s right, mother. Let’s have some, an a
bite, too, for I’m off again immediately.”
“ Yer won’t be stayin’ the night, then?”
“No. I’ve a sick-call at Bull-bull Station, and
I must start again at once and get there to-night. ”
“ I’m sorry for yer hurry, father, for there’s
some business for yer here. ” _
“ Indade ! And what moight it be ? ”
“ Well, father, there’s Mary. She be near her
toime to Ted Hogan, at Howlong; an’ they want’s
joinin’.”
“ Call ’em in, mother.”
Mrs. Dargan went out of the parlor, and soon
came back followed by Mary and her lover, a hard-
faced young boundary-rider, who, fortunately for
himself and the object of his affections, had ridden
over to the Squatters’ Rest that morning.
“So,” said the priest, sternly, “ so, you’ve not
had the dacency to wait my cornin’ ? ”
The young couple made no reply.
“ How do you expect to obtain a blessin’,” con-
tinued the priest, “when you stale the joys of
matrimony widout God’s leave? Kneel down, the
pair of you. Now, say the words afther me. Mrs.
Dargan, call all hands in as witnesses.”
For some minutes the ceremony proceeded; then
the priest asked, “ Mary O’Neill, do you take this
man for your husband ? ”
“ Yer ain’t asked ’im if ’e’ll ’ave me first,
Father.” . TT
“ Silence, you hussy. Will you have him < He
is going to marry you.”
“Yes, yer Riverince.”
“ That’s right. There now, you are man and
wife, and God bless you ! ”
“ Whisky all round, mother,” cried Ted Hogan,
and all joined in the toast which followed.
“ Is there anything else now, Mrs. Dargan ? ”
asked the priest,'pocketing the pound-note given
him by the newly-wedded man.
“ Yis, yer Riverence ; there’s a baptism.”
“Whose is the infant?”
“It’s Jane’s, father.”
“ Jane ! She’s not married ! ”
“Ah ! poor thing, father, she’s in a bad way,
an’ her man won’t be here this side Christmas.”
“ Who is he ? ”
“ Alick MTntyre, the bullocky.”
“ Heavens ! the sin, the sin of it all ! Fetch the
mother and child in at once.” _ .
A pale young woman was brought in, bearing m
her arms a fine little boy a month or two old.
“ Ah ! Jane, you’ve been in a devil of a hurry,”
said the priest, not unkindly. “ Who stand
sponsors for this child ? God bless him ! He’s a
beauty, too.” . ,
A toothless old raobiter in for a spree, and a
young fellow from a neighboring selection, stepped
forward, and the ceremony was soon over.
“ There, Jane, my girl, the little one is as pure
as snow now. But what’s all these tears for ?
Mrs. Dargan, what’s wrong ? ”
The women-folk, as if by magic, had all begun
to sob ; and the men stood here and there convers-
ing in whispers and looking very glum.
“Come now, mother, what’s the maning of it
all ? ” asked the priest again, and impatiently this
time.
“ Ah ! father, there’s pretty Nellie yet.”
“ An’ what, in heaven’s name, is wrong with
her—the angel of the flock? The pride of the
Big Gum Plain. The flower among so many
weeds.”
There was a ring of alarm in the old man’s
tones, and he looked anxiously from one to the
other.
“ Faith, father, it’s buryin’ she wants.”
“ Burying ? Nellie dead ! No, no. So bright,
so fair. The queen of you all ! Not dead ! ”
“ Indade, father.”
“ Take me to her.”
“ She’s all nicely laid out, wid a pretty coffin,
too, made by Ted, here. The poor lamb died
yester-morn.”
Mrs. Dargan led the priest into an adjoining
room where, on a stretcher, lay the body of poor
Nellie. She had been a very beautiful girl, and
even Death could not rob her perfect features of
their charm. The long golden hair had been care-
fully brushed and trained down each side of the
reclining figure. On her breast was a bunch of
wild flowers.
“ She’s bin ailin’ since your last visit, father.
The young gentleman from Mooraboo run was
after her, and Nellie was very fond of him. But
the dam blackguard, savin’ your presence, father,
wint away an’ got spliced to a lady in Adelaide,
an’ our girl here broke her poor heart an’ died. ”
Mrs. Dargan told her tale with many sobs.
“ And was she innocent, do you think, mother ? ”
asked the priest anxiously.
“ More’s the pity, no, Father. She ”
“God, God! The sin, the sin! Poor lamb,
to be wrecked by that son of the devil. Wait
until 1 meet him, which, plase Heaven, will be
soon ! Now bring all hands in, Mrs. Dargan, and
I’ll say prayers.”
And when it was over he spoke to them about
the savage way they were living, and said the
back-blocks should be called the Black-blocks, for
there was no light there. And an hour later,
after Nellie’s body had been placed in the rough
grave prepared for it, Father Connolly took his
leave of the shanty and its inmates, blessing them
all from his seat in the saddle. And as he rode
away a tear trickled down his face. “ Lord !” he
cried, “when will women know men?”
ALEX, ALLEN.
The North Melt), and Prahran branches of
the A.N.A. met the other night to contest the
final heat of a talking tournament. Subject :
“ Should Military Service be Compulsory in Aus-
tralia.” The judges were Sir Righteous Wrixon,
Dr. Embling, M.L.C., and a third gentleman who
is not yet renowned for blue toryism. North
Melbourne argued against compulsory soldiering,
and talked rings round the other fellows. Then
Wrixon and Embling wagged their heads vigor-
ously at the third judge, and the chairman decided
that Prahran had won by 18 points. Which
clearly proves that Australia must have a standing
army or perish—in the opinion of the patriot who
passed a Bill prohibiting radical Sunday news-
papers in Victoria. The Bulletin would like to
judge a debate on the question whether Wrixons
and Emblings are a worse disease for a young
country than small-pox.
A Licensed Pub.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I had come about twenty miles from the station,
and, reaching the top of the range, was reining in
my horse to listen to the music of the bell-birds
when the warning of the station hand came back
to me vividly : “ As the wind’s favorable, you 11
probably smell it on the top of the big range ;
that’s three miles away. ” , ,
“ It ” became more and more obvious as the
three miles turned to two, and the two to one.
Several tracks doubled in and out through the
trees of the sandy flat, and crossed each other here
and there. Earth, trees, and air were tainted.
The truth came to me : “ It ” comprised not only
a pub., but an extensive piggery. The grunting
and squealing drowned all other voices of nature
within a half-mile radius.
The pub. was of slabs, bark-and-log roofed. A
big boar was half asleep on the verandah ; a litter
was disporting in a large hole wherein a team had
sunk up to the axle a few days before and had
been dug out. I tied my horse to a verandah-post
and read the sign over the bar-door, “
— , to sell fermented and spirituous
liquors.” The bar which drinkers breasted was
painted white, and across its front and rudely
printed with a tar-brush was : “ I am a bit of a
liar meself, but ”
On the bar-floor sat a great, muscular ruffian,
naked but for a pair of trousers and one heavy
blucher boot. Pie looked so filthy that he might
have just come in from wallowing in the piggery.
He took no notice of my entrance, but with his
naked left foot in his lap and a butcher’s knife in
his right hand he went on cutting his toe-nails.
“ Where’s the boss ? ” I asked.
The animal raised a grisly face with matted,
filthy hair and beard, and looking out from two
small, cruel, bleary eyes, vented a half-drunken
grunt —“Can’t you blanky well see?”
“ Oh, you’re the boss ! I want a drink.”
The Boss arose, stuck his knife into a slab, and
went behind the bar. “ Whatelyouav ? ” His
tone and manner gave me to understand that his
stock was comprehensive and first-class.
‘ ‘ Lager beer ! ”
“ Ranhoutalager a week ago—won’t ’ave none
till the teams come back.”
“ Bottled English beer ! ”
“ Got no beer; sent last case away two days
hago to the station; big picnic’shon there. ”
“ Wine, then ! ”
“ Sent the last bottle to a sick selector’s wife last
night. ”
“Soda, lemonade !”
“ No call for them soft tack hout ’ere.”
He had only the dosed nicotine stuff—some of
it called rum, the rest whisky.
I asked him to have a whisky with me, and gave
him half-a-crown. We gulped down the liquid
lightning.
He kept the half-crown in the till. “ ’Ave
hanother,”he said. We had; he kept the half-
crown.
Suddenly loud words, followed almost instantly
by a fierce scuffle, burst upon us from the next
room. The publican rushed from behind his bar
and opened a side-door. On the floor of the room
two shearers, who had been playing cards on a
whisky-case, were struggling savagely. Their
drink-mad eyes were starting from their sockets ;
their teeth were clenched with fury ; white froth
was on one man’s lips. This madman grasped a
large, open clasp-knife, but the other held his
wrist in the grip of a vyce. The burly publican
dashed forward and kicked the man with the knife
on the point of the jaw, stretching him out sense-
less. Then he kicked the other in the ribs, with
such force that the wretch nearly went through
the door. Springing to his feet with a loud cry
of agony and fear, he ran out upon the track,
staggering like a mortally-wounded animal. After
going 50 yards his legs gave way, and he came
down with a thud. The publican took the uncon-
scious one from off the door by the nape of the
neck and seat of the trousers, and Hung him out
some yards in front of the bar. Then, standing
in the'bar door-way, with his arms folded across
liis Herculean chest, the boss of the shanty roared
in a voice of iron : “ I’ll ’ave harder in my ’ouse,
an’ no blanky fightin’.”

How’s That.
The “ Lormer’s Board” enquiry main-
tained its reputation for revelations and humor
right up to the finish. “Beak” Bird, the clown
of the show, weighed in with a speech for his own
defence, in which he stated that, after receiving
the magisterial toga from the hands of the chair-
man’s revered sire, he rambled out into the Rich-
mond paddock, or somewhere, and meditated on
the duties of his position. After much meditation
he decided to become the champion of young girls
who might be tempted by Aspasia to their moral
death, and he has been championing them accord-
ingly. Barrister Maxwell, on behalf of the Crown,
didn’t think it worth while to show cause why
this nice old gentleman should be shoved off the
Bench, because the necessity for his immediate
removal was sticking out a mile. Mr. Maxwell,
however, went to a good deal of trouble in attack-
ing Lormer, the man for whom he had practically
held a Crown brief throughout the proceedings.
Having duly enlarged upon the unfitness of teeto-
tal Lormer to adjudicate in hotel cases, the strictly
impartial Maxwell turned round and administered
a very nasty jar to the Crown Law Office, from
which he got his brief. Maxwell, on this matter,
was very interesting. Hear him! “The Minister
of Justice called upon Mr. Lormer to supply him
with particulars of the matters (Bench scandals)
referred to, and, as a result, the Board was ap-
pointed. Objections had been raised to the
higgledy-piggledy manner in which the case was
presented, but, in justice to himself, he (Maxwell)
must say that the initial dilatoriness on the part
of the Crown had seriously hampered him through-
out the whole of the enquiry. He had been work-
ing against time all the while. ”

Mr. Alfred 0. Jolliffs, of Granville, So. Australia, whose portrait we
give above, writes:
“I used Ayer’s Sarsaparilla in England for years before coming to
Australia. At times I was much troubled with boils and other eruptions
of the skin, caused, the doctors told me, by the running down of my
system and general debility. The only medicine that has ever done me
lasting good is Ayer's Sarsaparilla, which has always cured me—purifying
my blood, and giving me new life and health and vigour.”
The wonderful success of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla in purifying the blood and
restoring health and strength to systems enfeebled by disease, or the enervate
